			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Financial crisis of 2007–2008</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">Financial crisis of 2007–08</a>)</span></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the financial crisis that peaked in 2008. For the global recession triggered by the financial crisis, see <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>financial crisis of 2007–2008</b>, also known as the <b>global financial crisis</b> and the <b>2008 financial crisis</b>, is considered by many economists to have been the worst <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis" title="Financial crisis">financial crisis</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>It began in 2007 with a crisis in the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage" class="mw-redirect" title="Subprime mortgage">subprime mortgage</a> market in the USA, and developed into a full-blown international <a href="/wiki/Banking_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Banking crisis">banking crisis</a> with the collapse of the investment bank Lehman Brothers on September 15, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Excessive risk taking by banks such as Lehman Brothers helped to magnify the financial impact globally.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[6]</a></sup> Massive <a href="/wiki/Bailout" title="Bailout">bail-outs</a> of financial institutions and other palliative monetary and fiscal policies were employed to prevent a possible collapse of the world's financial system. The crisis was nonetheless followed by a global <a href="/wiki/Economic_downturn" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic downturn">economic downturn</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a>. The <a href="/wiki/European_debt_crisis" title="European debt crisis">European debt crisis</a>, a crisis in the banking system of the European countries using the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a>, followed later.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" title="Dodd–Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd–Frank</a> Act,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> was enacted in the US in the aftermath of the crisis to "promote the financial stability of the United States"<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> (although it has been criticized as the "biggest expansion of government power over banking and markets since the Depression"<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> creating new market distortions that might create new crises in the future,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and that it "has crushed small banks, restricted access to credit, and planted the seeds of financial instability"<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Basel III</a> capital and liquidity standards were adopted by countries around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Summary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subprime_mortgage_bubble">Subprime mortgage bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Subprime mortgage bubble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The precipitating factor was a high default rate in the United States <a href="/wiki/Subprime_lending" title="Subprime lending">subprime</a> home mortgage sector. The expansion of this sector was encouraged by the <a href="/wiki/Community_Reinvestment_Act" title="Community Reinvestment Act">Community Reinvestment Act</a> (CRA),<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> a US federal law designed to help low- and moderate-income Americans get mortgage loans.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Many of these subprime (high risk) loans were then bundled and sold, finally accruing to quasi-government agencies (<a href="/wiki/Fannie_Mae" title="Fannie Mae">Fannie Mae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie Mac</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The implicit guarantee by the US federal government created a <a href="/wiki/Moral_hazard" title="Moral hazard">moral hazard</a> and contributed to a glut of risky lending. Many of these loans were also bundled together and formed into new financial instruments called <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_securities" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortgage-backed securities">mortgage-backed securities</a>, which could be sold as (ostensibly) low-risk securities partly because they were often backed by <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swaps" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit default swaps">credit default swaps</a> insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Because mortgage lenders could pass these mortgages (and the associated risks) on in this way, they could and did adopt loose underwriting criteria (encouraged by regulators), and some developed aggressive lending practices. The accumulation and subsequent high default rate of these mortgages led to the financial crisis, and the consequent damage to the world economy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Banking_crisis">Banking crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Banking crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>High mortgage approval rates led to a large pool of home-buyers, which drove up housing prices. This appreciation in value led large numbers of homeowners (subprime or not) to borrow against their homes as an apparent windfall. This "bubble" would be burst by a rising Single-Family Residential Mortgages Delinquency Rate (beginning in August, 2006 and peaking in the first quarter, 2010).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The high delinquency rates led to a rapid devaluation of financial instruments (mortgage-backed securities including bundled loan portfolios, derivatives and Credit Default Swaps). As the value of these assets plummeted, the market (buyers) for these securities evaporated and banks who were heavily invested in these assets began to experience a liquidity crisis. Lehman Brothers filed for bankruptcy on September 15, 2008. Merrill Lynch, AIG, Freddie Mac, Fannie Mae, HBOS, Royal Bank of Scotland, Bradford &amp; Bingley, Fortis, Hypo and Alliance &amp; Leicester were all expected to follow – with a US federal bailout announced the following day beginning with $85B to AIG. In spite of trillions<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> paid out by the US federal government, it became much more difficult to borrow money. The resulting decrease in buyers caused housing prices to plummet.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the collapse of large financial institutions was prevented by the <a href="/wiki/Bailout" title="Bailout">bailout</a> of banks by national governments, <a href="/wiki/Stock_market" title="Stock market">stock markets</a> still dropped worldwide. In many areas, the housing market also suffered, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Eviction" title="Eviction">evictions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreclosure" title="Foreclosure">foreclosures</a>, and prolonged unemployment. The crisis played a significant role in the failure of key businesses, declines in consumer wealth estimated in trillions of US dollars, and a downturn in economic activity leading to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> of 2008–2012 and contributing to the <a href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">European sovereign-debt crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The active phase of the crisis, which manifested as a <a href="/wiki/Liquidity_crisis" title="Liquidity crisis">liquidity crisis</a>, can be dated from August 9, 2007, when <a href="/wiki/BNP_Paribas" title="BNP Paribas">BNP Paribas</a> terminated withdrawals from three hedge funds citing "a complete evaporation of liquidity".<sup id="cite_ref-G8512_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G8512-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The bursting of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_housing_bubble" title="United States housing bubble">US housing bubble</a>, which peaked at the end of 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-USCensus-new-housing_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus-new-housing-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Census_home_ownership_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_home_ownership-26">[26]</a></sup> caused the values of <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> tied to US <a href="/wiki/Real_estate_pricing" class="mw-redirect" title="Real estate pricing">real estate pricing</a> to plummet, damaging financial institutions globally.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup> The financial crisis was triggered by a complex interplay of policies that encouraged home ownership, providing easier access to loans for subprime borrowers, overvaluation of bundled subprime mortgages based on the theory that housing prices would continue to escalate, questionable trading practices on behalf of both buyers and sellers, compensation structures that prioritize short-term deal flow over long-term value creation, and a lack of adequate capital holdings from banks and insurance companies to back the financial commitments they were making.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-papers.ssrn_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers.ssrn-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Questions regarding bank <a href="/wiki/Solvency" title="Solvency">solvency</a>, declines in credit availability, and damaged investor confidence affected global stock markets, where securities suffered large losses during 2008 and early 2009. Economies worldwide slowed during this period, as credit tightened and international trade declined.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Governments and <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">central banks</a> responded with unprecedented <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_policy" title="Fiscal policy">fiscal stimulus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a> expansion and institutional bailouts.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In the US, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act_of_2009" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009">American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many causes for the financial crisis have been suggested, with varying weight assigned by experts.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The US Senate's <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_and_the_Financial_Crisis:_Anatomy_of_a_Financial_Collapse" title="Wall Street and the Financial Crisis: Anatomy of a Financial Collapse">Levin–Coburn Report</a> concluded that the crisis was the result of "high risk, complex financial products; undisclosed conflicts of interest; the failure of regulators, the credit rating agencies, and the market itself to rein in the excesses of Wall Street."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> concluded that the financial crisis was avoidable and was caused by "widespread failures in <a href="/wiki/Financial_regulation" title="Financial regulation">financial regulation</a> and supervision", "dramatic failures of <a href="/wiki/Corporate_governance" title="Corporate governance">corporate governance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a> at many systemically important <a href="/wiki/Financial_institutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial institutions">financial institutions</a>", "a combination of excessive borrowing, risky investments, and lack of transparency" by financial institutions, ill preparation and inconsistent action by government that "added to the uncertainty and panic", a "systemic breakdown in accountability and ethics", "collapsing mortgage-lending standards and the mortgage securitization pipeline", deregulation of <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_(finance)" title="Over-the-counter (finance)">over-the-counter</a> <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a>, and "the failures of credit rating agencies" to correctly price risk.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Gramm-Leach-Bliley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act">1999 repeal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Glass-Steagall_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass-Steagall Act">Glass-Steagall Act</a> effectively removed the separation between <a href="/wiki/Investment_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment bank">investment banks</a> and depository banks in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Critics argued that <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agency" title="Credit rating agency">credit rating agencies</a> and investors failed to accurately price the <a href="/wiki/Financial_risk" title="Financial risk">risk</a> involved with <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_loan" title="Mortgage loan">mortgage</a>-related financial products, and that governments did not adjust their regulatory practices to address 21st-century financial markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Declaration_of_G20_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Declaration_of_G20-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>Research into the causes of the financial crisis has also focused on the role of interest rate spreads.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fair_value_accounting_and_the_subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Fair value accounting and the subprime mortgage crisis">Fair value accounting</a> was issued as US accounting standard <a href="/wiki/SFAS_157" title="SFAS 157">SFAS 157</a> in 2006 by the privately run <a href="/wiki/Financial_Accounting_Standards_Board" title="Financial Accounting Standards Board">Financial Accounting Standards Board</a> (FASB)—delegated by the SEC with the task of establishing financial reporting standards.<sup id="cite_ref-wharton_2009_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wharton_2009-41">[41]</a></sup> This required that tradable assets such as <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage securities</a> be valued according to their current market value rather than their historic cost or some future expected value. When the market for such securities became volatile and collapsed, the resulting loss of value had a major financial effect upon the institutions holding them even if they had no immediate plans to sell them.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_the_Great_Recession" title="Causes of the Great Recession">Causes of the Great Recession</a></div>
<p>The immediate cause or trigger of the crisis was the bursting of the US housing bubble, which peaked in 2006/2007.<sup id="cite_ref-USCensus-new-housing_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus-new-housing-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Census_home_ownership_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_home_ownership-26">[26]</a></sup> Already-rising default rates on "<a href="/wiki/Subprime" class="mw-redirect" title="Subprime">subprime</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate mortgages</a> (ARM) began to increase quickly thereafter.</p>
<p>Easy availability of credit in the US, fueled by large inflows of foreign funds after the <a href="/wiki/1998_Russian_financial_crisis" title="1998 Russian financial crisis">Russian debt crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">Asian financial crisis</a> of the 1997–1998 period, led to a housing construction boom and facilitated debt-financed consumer spending. As banks began to give out more loans to potential home owners, housing prices began to rise. Lax lending standards and rising real estate prices also contributed to the real estate bubble. Loans of various types (e.g., mortgage, credit card, and auto) were easy to obtain and consumers assumed an unprecedented debt load.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>As part of the housing and credit booms, the number of financial agreements called <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> (MBS) and <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDO), which derived their value from mortgage payments and housing prices, greatly increased.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup> Such <a href="/wiki/Financial_innovation" title="Financial innovation">financial innovation</a> enabled institutions and investors around the world to invest in the US housing market. As housing prices declined, major global financial institutions that had borrowed and invested heavily in subprime MBS reported significant losses.<sup id="cite_ref-IMF_Loss_Estimates_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_Loss_Estimates-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Falling prices also resulted in homes worth less than the mortgage loan, providing a financial incentive to enter <a href="/wiki/Foreclosure" title="Foreclosure">foreclosure</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="if the outcome of foreclosure for the lender would be a financial loss compared with the paper value of the mortgage, why would this be a financial incentive to foreclose? (September 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The ongoing foreclosure epidemic that began in late 2006 in the US and only reduced to historical levels in early 2014<sup id="cite_ref-RealtyTrac_Foreclosure_Rates_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RealtyTrac_Foreclosure_Rates-47">[47]</a></sup> drained significant wealth from consumers, losing up to $4.2 trillion<sup id="cite_ref-Estimate_of_Household_Wealth_lost_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Estimate_of_Household_Wealth_lost-48">[48]</a></sup> in wealth from <a href="/wiki/Home_equity" title="Home equity">home equity</a>. Defaults and losses on other loan types also increased significantly as the crisis expanded from the housing market to other parts of the economy. Total losses are estimated in the trillions of US dollars globally.<sup id="cite_ref-IMF_Loss_Estimates_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IMF_Loss_Estimates-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg/350px-NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg" width="350" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg/525px-NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg/700px-NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="838" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NYUGDPFinancialShare.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Share in GDP of US financial sector since 1860<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the housing and credit bubbles were building, a series of factors caused the financial system to both expand and become increasingly fragile, a process called <a href="/wiki/Financialization" title="Financialization">financialization</a>. US Government policy from the 1970s onward has emphasized <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> to encourage business, which resulted in less oversight of activities and less disclosure of information about new activities undertaken by banks and other evolving financial institutions. Thus, policymakers did not immediately recognize the increasingly important role played by financial institutions such as investment banks and <a href="/wiki/Hedge_funds" class="mw-redirect" title="Hedge funds">hedge funds</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a>. Some experts believe these institutions had become as important as commercial (depository) banks in providing credit to the US economy, but they were not subject to the same regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-newyorkfed.org_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorkfed.org-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>These institutions, as well as certain regulated banks, had also assumed significant debt burdens while providing the loans described above and did not have a financial cushion sufficient to absorb large loan defaults or MBS losses.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> These losses affected the ability of financial institutions to lend, slowing economic activity. Concerns regarding the stability of key financial institutions drove central banks to provide funds to encourage lending and restore faith in the <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> markets, which are integral to funding business operations. Governments also <a href="/wiki/Bailout" title="Bailout">bailed out</a> key financial institutions and implemented economic stimulus programs, assuming significant additional financial commitments.</p>
<p>The US <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> reported its findings in January 2011. It concluded that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>the crisis was avoidable and was caused by: widespread failures in financial regulation, including the Federal Reserve's failure to stem the tide of <a href="/wiki/Toxic_asset" title="Toxic asset">toxic mortgages</a>; dramatic breakdowns in corporate governance including too many financial firms acting recklessly and taking on too much risk; an explosive mix of excessive borrowing and risk by households and Wall Street that put the financial system on a collision course with crisis; key policy makers ill prepared for the crisis, lacking a full understanding of the financial system they oversaw; and systemic breaches in accountability and ethics at all levels.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subprime_lending">Subprime lending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Subprime lending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">Subprime mortgage crisis</a></div>
<p>The 2000s were the decade of subprime borrowers; no longer was this a segment left to fringe lenders. The relaxing of credit lending standards by investment banks and commercial banks drove this about-face. Subprime did not become magically less risky; Wall Street just accepted this higher risk.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> During a period of tough competition between mortgage lenders for revenue and market share, and when the supply of creditworthy borrowers was limited, mortgage lenders relaxed underwriting standards and originated riskier mortgages to less creditworthy borrowers.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup> In the view of some analysts, the relatively conservative <a href="/wiki/Government-sponsored_enterprise" title="Government-sponsored enterprise">government-sponsored enterprises</a> (GSEs) policed mortgage originators and maintained relatively high underwriting standards prior to 2003. However, as market power shifted from securitizers to originators and as intense competition from private securitizers undermined GSE power, mortgage standards declined and risky loans proliferated.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup> The worst loans were originated in 2004–2007, the years of the most intense competition between securitizers and the lowest market share for the GSEs.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png/350px-U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png/525px-U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png/700px-U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Home_Ownership_and_Subprime_Origination_Share.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US subprime lending expanded dramatically 2004–2006</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As well as easy credit conditions, there is evidence that competitive pressures contributed to an increase in the amount of subprime lending during the years preceding the crisis. Major US investment banks and GSEs such as <a href="/wiki/Fannie_Mae" title="Fannie Mae">Fannie Mae</a> played an important role in the expansion of lending, with GSEs eventually relaxing their standards to try to catch up with the private banks.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>A contrarian view is that Fannie Mae and <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie Mac</a> led the way to relaxed underwriting standards, starting in 1995, by advocating the use of easy-to-qualify automated underwriting and appraisal systems, by designing the no-downpayment products issued by lenders, by the promotion of thousands of small mortgage brokers, and by their close relationship to subprime loan aggregators such as <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Home_Loans" title="Bank of America Home Loans">Countrywide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Graham_Fisher_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graham_Fisher-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Depending on how “subprime” mortgages are defined, they remained below 10% of all mortgage originations until 2004, when they spiked to nearly 20% and remained there through the 2005–2006 peak of the United States housing bubble.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority report of the Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission, written by the six Democratic appointees, the minority report, written by three of the four Republican appointees, studies by Federal Reserve economists, and the work of several independent scholars generally contend that government affordable housing policy was not the primary cause of the financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup> Although they concede that governmental policies had some role in causing the crisis, they contend that GSE loans performed better than loans securitized by private investment banks, and performed better than some loans originated by institutions that held loans in their own portfolios.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his dissent to the majority report of the Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission, <a href="/wiki/American_Enterprise_Institute" title="American Enterprise Institute">American Enterprise Institute</a> <a href="/wiki/Fellow" title="Fellow">fellow</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_J._Wallison" title="Peter J. Wallison">Peter J. Wallison</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> stated his belief that the roots of the financial crisis can be traced directly and primarily to affordable housing policies initiated by the US Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD) in the 1990s and to massive risky loan purchases by government-sponsored entities Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. Later, based upon information in the SEC's December 2011 securities fraud case against six former executives of Fannie and Freddie, Peter Wallison and Edward Pinto estimated that, in 2008, Fannie and Freddie held 13 million substandard loans totaling over $2 trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early and mid-2000s, the Bush administration called numerous times<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> for investigation into the safety and soundness of the GSEs and their swelling portfolio of subprime mortgages. On September 10, 2003, the House Financial Services Committee held a hearing at the urging of the administration to assess safety and soundness issues and to review a recent report by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Federal_Housing_Enterprise_Oversight" title="Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight">Office of Federal Housing Enterprise Oversight</a> (OFHEO) that had uncovered accounting discrepancies within the two entities.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The hearings never resulted in new legislation or formal investigation of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, as many of the committee members refused to accept the report and instead rebuked OFHEO for their attempt at regulation.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Some believe this was an early warning to the systemic risk that the growing market in subprime mortgages posed to the US financial system that went unheeded.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2000 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">United States Department of the Treasury</a> study of lending trends for 305 cities from 1993 to 1998 showed that $467&#160;billion of mortgage lending was made by <a href="/wiki/Community_Reinvestment_Act" title="Community Reinvestment Act">Community Reinvestment Act</a> (CRA)-covered lenders into low and mid level income (LMI) borrowers and neighborhoods, representing 10% of all US mortgage lending during the period. The majority of these were prime loans. Sub-prime loans made by CRA-covered institutions constituted a 3% market share of LMI loans in 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> but in the run-up to the crisis, fully 25% of all sub-prime lending occurred at CRA-covered institutions and another 25% of sub-prime loans had some connection with CRA.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Furthermore, most sub-prime loans were not made to the LMI borrowers targeted by the CRA, especially in the years 2005–2006 leading up to the crisis, nor did it find any evidence that lending under the CRA rules increased delinquency rates or that the CRA indirectly influenced independent mortgage lenders to ramp up sub-prime lending.</p>
<p>To other analysts the delay between CRA rule changes (in 1995) and the explosion of subprime lending is not surprising, and does not exonerate the CRA. They contend that there were two, connected causes to the crisis: the relaxation of underwriting standards in 1995 and the ultra-low interest rates initiated by the Federal Reserve after the terrorist attack on September 11, 2001. Both causes had to be in place before the crisis could take place.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Critics also point out that publicly announced CRA loan commitments were massive, totaling $4.5 trillion in the years between 1994 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> They also argue that the Federal Reserve's classification of CRA loans as "prime" is based on the faulty and self-serving assumption that high-interest-rate loans (3 percentage points over average) equal "subprime" loans.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Others have pointed out that there were not enough of these loans made to cause a crisis of this magnitude. In an article in Portfolio Magazine, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis" title="Michael Lewis">Michael Lewis</a> spoke with one trader who noted that "There weren't enough Americans with [bad] credit taking out [bad loans] to satisfy investors' appetite for the end product." Essentially, investment banks and hedge funds used financial innovation to enable large wagers to be made, far beyond the actual value of the underlying mortgage loans, using <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a> called credit default swaps, collateralized debt obligations and synthetic CDOs.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of March 2011, the FDIC had had to pay out $9 billion to cover losses on bad loans at 165 failed financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-post_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-post-72">[72]</a></sup> The Congressional Budget Office estimated, in June 2011, that the bailout to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac exceeds $300 billion (calculated by adding the fair value deficits of the entities to the direct bailout funds at the time).<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> argued in January 2010 that the simultaneous growth of the residential and commercial real estate pricing bubbles and the global nature of the crisis undermines the case made by those who argue that Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac, CRA, or predatory lending were primary causes of the crisis. In other words, bubbles in both markets developed even though only the residential market was affected by these potential causes.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Countering Krugman, Peter J. Wallison wrote: "It is not true that every bubble—even a large bubble—has the potential to cause a financial crisis when it deflates." Wallison notes that other developed countries had "large bubbles during the 1997–2007 period" but "the losses associated with mortgage delinquencies and defaults when these bubbles deflated were far lower than the losses suffered in the United States when the 1997–2007 [bubble] deflated." According to Wallison, the reason the US <i>residential housing</i> bubble (as opposed to other types of bubbles) led to financial crisis was that it was supported by a huge number of substandard loans—generally with low or no downpayments.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallison_1_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallison_1-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Krugman's contention (that the growth of a commercial real estate bubble indicates that US housing policy was not the cause of the crisis) is challenged by additional analysis. After researching the default of commercial loans during the financial crisis, Xudong An and Anthony B. Sanders reported (in December 2010): "We find limited evidence that substantial deterioration in CMBS [commercial mortgage-backed securities] loan underwriting occurred prior to the crisis."<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Other analysts support the contention that the crisis in commercial real estate and related lending took place <i>after</i> the crisis in residential real estate. Business journalist Kimberly Amadeo reports: "The first signs of decline in residential real estate occurred in 2006. Three years later, commercial real estate started feeling the effects.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Denice A. Gierach, a real estate attorney and CPA, wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>...most of the commercial real estate loans were good loans destroyed by a really bad economy. In other words, the borrowers did not cause the loans to go bad, it was the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_the_housing_bubble">Growth of the housing bubble</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Growth of the housing bubble">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_housing_bubble" title="United States housing bubble">United States housing bubble</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png/290px-Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png" width="290" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png/435px-Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png/580px-Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png 2x" data-file-width="921" data-file-height="438" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Median_and_Average_Sales_Prices_of_New_Homes_Sold_in_United_States_1963-2008_annual.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A graph showing the median and average sales prices of new homes sold in the United States between 1963 and 2008 (not adjusted for inflation)<sup id="cite_ref-USCensus-new-housing_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCensus-new-housing-25">[25]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Between 1998 and 2006, the price of the typical American house increased by 124%.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> During the two decades ending in 2001, the national median home price ranged from 2.9 to 3.1 times median household income. This ratio rose to 4.0 in 2004, and 4.6 in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-businessweek1_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-80">[80]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Housing_bubble" class="mw-redirect" title="Housing bubble">housing bubble</a> resulted in many homeowners refinancing their homes at lower interest rates, or financing consumer spending by taking out <a href="/wiki/Second_mortgage" title="Second mortgage">second mortgages</a> secured by the price appreciation.</p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Peabody_Award" title="Peabody Award">Peabody Award</a> winning program, <a href="/wiki/National_Public_Radio" class="mw-redirect" title="National Public Radio">NPR</a> correspondents argued that a "Giant Pool of Money" (represented by $70&#160;trillion in worldwide fixed income investments) sought higher yields than those offered by US Treasury bonds early in the decade. This pool of money had roughly doubled in size from 2000 to 2007, yet the supply of relatively safe, income generating investments had not grown as fast. Investment banks on Wall Street answered this demand with products such as the <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed security</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligation</a> that were assigned safe <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating" title="Credit rating">ratings</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Credit_rating_agencies_and_the_subprime_crisis" title="Credit rating agencies and the subprime crisis">credit rating agencies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-This_American_Life_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_American_Life-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In effect, Wall Street connected this pool of money to the mortgage market in the US, with enormous fees accruing to those throughout the mortgage <a href="/wiki/Supply_chain" title="Supply chain">supply chain</a>, from the mortgage broker selling the loans to small banks that funded the brokers and the large investment banks behind them. By approximately 2003, the supply of mortgages originated at traditional lending standards had been exhausted, and continued strong demand began to drive down lending standards.<sup id="cite_ref-This_American_Life_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_American_Life-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The collateralized debt obligation in particular enabled financial institutions to obtain investor funds to finance subprime and other lending, extending or increasing the housing bubble and generating large fees. This essentially places cash payments from multiple mortgages or other debt obligations into a single pool from which specific securities draw in a specific sequence of priority. Those securities first in line received investment-grade ratings from rating agencies. Securities with lower priority had lower credit ratings but theoretically a higher rate of return on the amount invested.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>By September 2008, average US housing prices had declined by over 20% from their mid-2006 peak.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> As prices declined, borrowers with <a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate mortgages</a> could not refinance to avoid the higher payments associated with rising interest rates and began to default. During 2007, lenders began foreclosure proceedings on nearly 1.3&#160;million properties, a 79% increase over 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This increased to 2.3&#160;million in 2008, an 81% increase vs. 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> By August 2008, 9.2% of all US mortgages outstanding were either delinquent or in foreclosure.<sup id="cite_ref-mbaa1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mbaa1-88">[88]</a></sup> By September 2009, this had risen to 14.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Easy_credit_conditions">Easy credit conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Easy credit conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lower interest rates encouraged borrowing. From 2000 to 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> lowered the <a href="/wiki/Federal_funds_rate" title="Federal funds rate">federal funds rate</a> target from 6.5% to 1.0%.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This was done to soften the effects of the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">dot-com bubble</a> and the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 2001 terrorist attacks</a>, as well as to combat a perceived risk of <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WallStreetJournal_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WallStreetJournal-91">[91]</a></sup> As early as 2002 it was apparent that credit was fueling housing instead of business investment as some economists went so far as to advocate that the Fed "needs to create a housing bubble to replace the Nasdaq bubble".<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Moreover, empirical studies using data from advanced countries show that excessive credit growth contributed greatly to the severity of the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png/350px-U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png/525px-U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png/700px-U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Trade_Deficit_Dollars_and_percentage_GDP.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US current account deficit.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Additional downward pressure on interest rates was created by the high and rising US <a href="/wiki/Current_account" title="Current account">current account</a> deficit, which peaked along with the housing bubble in 2006. Federal Reserve chairman <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bernanke" title="Ben Bernanke">Ben Bernanke</a> explained how trade deficits required the US to borrow money from abroad, in the process bidding up bond prices and lowering interest rates.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bernanke explained that between 1996 and 2004, the US current account deficit increased by $650&#160;billion, from 1.5% to 5.8% of GDP. Financing these deficits required the country to borrow large sums from abroad, much of it from countries running trade surpluses. These were mainly the emerging economies in Asia and oil-exporting nations. The <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_payments" title="Balance of payments">balance of payments</a> <a href="/wiki/Identity_(mathematics)" title="Identity (mathematics)">identity</a> requires that a country (such as the US) running a <a href="/wiki/Current_account" title="Current account">current account</a> deficit also have a <a href="/wiki/Capital_account" title="Capital account">capital account</a> (investment) surplus of the same amount. Hence large and growing amounts of foreign funds (capital) flowed into the US to finance its imports.</p>
<p>All of this created demand for various types of financial assets, raising the prices of those assets while lowering interest rates. Foreign investors had these funds to lend either because they had very high personal savings rates (as high as 40% in China) or because of high oil prices. Ben Bernanke has referred to this as a "<a href="/wiki/Saving_glut" class="mw-redirect" title="Saving glut">saving glut</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Bernanke_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernanke-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>A flood of funds (<a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">capital</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquidity" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquidity">liquidity</a>) reached the US financial markets. Foreign governments supplied funds by purchasing <a href="/wiki/Treasury_bond" class="mw-redirect" title="Treasury bond">Treasury bonds</a> and thus avoided much of the direct effect of the crisis. US households, on the other hand, used funds borrowed from foreigners to finance consumption or to bid up the prices of housing and financial assets. Financial institutions invested foreign funds in <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>.</p>
<p>The Fed then raised the Fed funds rate significantly between July 2004 and July 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> This contributed to an increase in 1-year and 5-year <a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate mortgage</a> (ARM) rates, making ARM interest rate resets more expensive for homeowners.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This may have also contributed to the deflating of the housing bubble, as asset prices generally move inversely to interest rates, and it became riskier to speculate in housing.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> US housing and financial assets dramatically declined in value after the housing bubble burst.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weak_and_fraudulent_underwriting_practices">Weak and fraudulent underwriting practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Weak and fraudulent underwriting practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Subprime lending standards declined: in the early 2000, a subprime borrower had a FICO score of 660 or less. By 2005, many lenders dropped the required FICO score to 620, making it much easier to qualify for prime loans and making subprime lending a riskier business. Proof of income and assets were de-emphasized. Loans moved from full documentation to low documentation to no documentation. One subprime mortgage product that gained wide acceptance was the no income, no job, no asset verification required (NINJA) mortgage. Informally, these loans were aptly referred to as "liar loans" because they encouraged borrowers to be less than honest in the loan application process.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Testimony given to the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> by Richard M. Bowen III on events during his tenure as the Business Chief Underwriter for Correspondent Lending in the Consumer Lending Group for <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a> (where he was responsible for over 220 professional underwriters) suggests that by the final years of the US housing bubble (2006–2007), the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_underwriting" title="Mortgage underwriting">mortgage underwriting</a> standards was endemic. His testimony stated that by 2006, 60% of mortgages purchased by <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citi</a> from some 1,600 mortgage companies were "defective" (were not underwritten to policy, or did not contain all policy-required documents)—this, despite the fact that each of these 1,600 originators was contractually responsible (certified via representations and warrantees) that its mortgage originations met <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citi's</a> standards. Moreover, during 2007, "defective mortgages (from mortgage originators contractually bound to perform underwriting to <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citi's</a> standards) increased... to over 80% of production".<sup id="cite_ref-FCICBowenCitiTestimony_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FCICBowenCitiTestimony-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>In separate testimony to Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission, officers of Clayton Holdings – the largest residential loan due diligence and securitization surveillance company in the United States and Europe—testified that Clayton's review of over 900,000 mortgages issued from January 2006 to June 2007 revealed that scarcely 54% of the loans met their originators’ underwriting standards. The analysis (conducted on behalf of 23 investment and commercial banks, including 7 "<a href="/wiki/Too_big_to_fail" title="Too big to fail">too big to fail</a>" banks) additionally showed that 28% of the sampled loans did not meet the minimal standards of any issuer. Clayton's analysis further showed that 39% of these loans (i.e. those not meeting <i>any</i> issuer's minimal underwriting standards) were subsequently securitized and sold to investors.<sup id="cite_ref-NewYorkTimes_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewYorkTimes-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ClaytonTrendingReport_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClaytonTrendingReport-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is strong evidence that the GSEs—due to their large size and market power—were far more effective at policing underwriting by originators and forcing underwriters to repurchase defective loans. By contrast, private securitizers have been far less aggressive and less effective in recovering losses from originators on behalf of investors.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predatory_lending">Predatory lending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Predatory lending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Predatory lending refers to the practice of unscrupulous lenders, enticing borrowers to enter into "unsafe" or "unsound" secured loans for inappropriate purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>A classic <a href="/wiki/Bait-and-switch" title="Bait-and-switch">bait-and-switch</a> method was used by <a href="/wiki/Countrywide_Financial" class="mw-redirect" title="Countrywide Financial">Countrywide Financial</a>, advertising low interest rates for home refinancing. Such loans were covered by very detailed contracts, and swapped for more expensive loan products on the day of closing. Whereas the advertisement might state that 1% or 1.5% interest would be charged, the consumer would be put into an adjustable rate mortgage (ARM) in which the interest charged would be greater than the mortgage payments, creating <a href="/wiki/Negative_amortization" title="Negative amortization">negative amortization</a> which the credit consumer might not notice until long after the loan transaction had been consummated.</p>
<p>Countrywide, sued by <a href="/wiki/California_Attorney_General" class="mw-redirect" title="California Attorney General">California Attorney General</a> Jerry Brown for "unfair business practices" and "false advertising" was making high cost mortgages "to homeowners with weak credit, adjustable rate mortgages (ARMs) that allowed homeowners to make interest-only payments".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> When housing prices decreased, homeowners in ARMs then had little incentive to pay their monthly payments, since their home equity had disappeared. This caused Countrywide's financial condition to deteriorate, ultimately resulting in a decision by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Thrift_Supervision" title="Office of Thrift Supervision">Office of Thrift Supervision</a> to seize the lender.</p>
<p>Former employees from <a href="/wiki/Ameriquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Ameriquest">Ameriquest</a>, which was United States' leading wholesale lender,<sup id="cite_ref-RoadToRuinMayANP09_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoadToRuinMayANP09-108">[108]</a></sup> described a system in which they were pushed to falsify mortgage documents and then sell the mortgages to Wall Street banks eager to make fast profits.<sup id="cite_ref-RoadToRuinMayANP09_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoadToRuinMayANP09-108">[108]</a></sup> There is growing evidence that such <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_fraud" title="Mortgage fraud">mortgage frauds</a> may be a cause of the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-RoadToRuinMayANP09_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RoadToRuinMayANP09-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deregulation">Deregulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Deregulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Government_policies_and_the_subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Government policies and the subprime mortgage crisis">Government policies and the subprime mortgage crisis</a></div>
<p>A 2012 OECD study<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> suggest that bank regulation based on the Basel accords encourage unconventional business practices and contributed to or even reinforced the financial crisis. In other cases, laws were changed or enforcement weakened in parts of the financial system. Key examples include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Depository_Institutions_Deregulation_and_Monetary_Control_Act" title="Depository Institutions Deregulation and Monetary Control Act">Depository Institutions Deregulation and Monetary Control Act</a> of 1980 (DIDMCA) phased out a number of restrictions on banks' financial practices, broadened their lending powers, allowed <a href="/wiki/Credit_union" title="Credit union">credit unions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan" class="mw-redirect" title="Savings and loan">savings and loans</a> to offer <a href="/wiki/Checkable_deposit" class="mw-redirect" title="Checkable deposit">checkable deposits</a>, and raised the <a href="/wiki/Deposit_insurance" title="Deposit insurance">deposit insurance</a> limit from $40,000 to $100,000 (thereby potentially lessening depositor scrutiny of lenders' risk management policies).<sup id="cite_ref-FDIC_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDIC-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li>In October 1982, US President Ronald Reagan signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Garn%E2%80%93St._Germain_Depository_Institutions_Act" title="Garn–St. Germain Depository Institutions Act">Garn–St. Germain Depository Institutions Act</a>, which provided for <a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate mortgage loans</a>, began the process of banking deregulation, and contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_crisis" title="Savings and loan crisis">savings and loan crisis</a> of the late 1980s/early 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li>In November 1999, US President Bill Clinton signed into law the <a href="/wiki/Gramm%E2%80%93Leach%E2%80%93Bliley_Act" title="Gramm–Leach–Bliley Act">Gramm–Leach–Bliley Act</a>, which repealed provisions of the Glass-Steagall Act that prohibit a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding company</a> from owning other financial companies. The repeal effectively removed the separation that previously existed between Wall Street investment banks and depository banks, providing a government stamp of approval for a universal risk-taking banking model. Investment banks such as Lehman would now be thrust into direct competition with commercial banks.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Most analysts say that this repeal directly contributed to the severity of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932010" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2010">Financial crisis of 2007–2010</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> However, there is perspective that repeal made little difference because the institutions that were greatly affected did not fall under the jurisdiction of the act itself.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2004, the US <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> relaxed the <a href="/wiki/Net_capital_rule" title="Net capital rule">net capital rule</a>, which enabled investment banks to substantially increase the level of debt they were taking on, fueling the growth in mortgage-backed securities supporting subprime mortgages. The SEC has conceded that self-regulation of investment banks contributed to the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>Financial institutions in the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a> are not subject to the same regulation as depository banks, allowing them to assume additional debt obligations relative to their financial cushion or capital base.<sup id="cite_ref-Krugman_2009_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krugman_2009-117">[117]</a></sup> This was the case despite the <a href="/wiki/Long-Term_Capital_Management" title="Long-Term Capital Management">Long-Term Capital Management</a> debacle in 1998, where a highly leveraged shadow institution failed with systemic implications.</li>
<li>Regulators and accounting standard-setters allowed depository banks such as <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a> to move significant amounts of assets and liabilities off-balance sheet into complex legal entities called <a href="/wiki/Structured_investment_vehicles" class="mw-redirect" title="Structured investment vehicles">structured investment vehicles</a>, masking the weakness of the capital base of the firm or degree of <a href="/wiki/Financial_leverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial leverage">leverage</a> or risk taken. One news agency estimated that the top four US banks will have to return between $500&#160;billion and $1&#160;trillion to their balance sheets during 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> This increased uncertainty during the crisis regarding the financial position of the major banks.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Off-balance sheet entities were also used by <a href="/wiki/Enron_scandal" title="Enron scandal">Enron</a> as part of the scandal that brought down that company in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>
<li>As early as 1997, Federal Reserve chairman <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a> fought to keep the derivatives market unregulated.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> With the advice of the <a href="/wiki/Working_Group_on_Financial_Markets" title="Working Group on Financial Markets">President's Working Group on Financial Markets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> the US Congress and President Bill Clinton allowed the self-regulation of the <a href="/wiki/Over-the-counter_(finance)" title="Over-the-counter (finance)">over-the-counter</a> derivatives market when they enacted the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Modernization_Act_of_2000" title="Commodity Futures Modernization Act of 2000">Commodity Futures Modernization Act of 2000</a>. Derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swaps" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit default swaps">credit default swaps</a> (CDS) can be used to hedge or speculate against particular credit risks without necessarily owning the underlying debt instruments. The volume of CDS outstanding increased 100-fold from 1998 to 2008, with estimates of the debt covered by CDS contracts, as of November 2008, ranging from US$33 to $47&#160;trillion. Total over-the-counter (OTC) derivative <a href="/wiki/Notional_value" class="mw-redirect" title="Notional value">notional value</a> rose to $683&#160;trillion by June 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Warren_Buffett" title="Warren Buffett">Warren Buffett</a> famously referred to derivatives as "financial weapons of mass destruction" in early 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_debt_burden_or_overleveraging">Increased debt burden or overleveraging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Increased debt burden or overleveraging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg/300px-Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg.png" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg/450px-Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg/600px-Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="675" data-file-height="450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leverage_ratios_for_major_investment_banks.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Leverage ratios of investment banks increased significantly between 2003 and 2007.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to the crisis, financial institutions became highly leveraged, increasing their appetite for risky investments and reducing their resilience in case of losses. Much of this leverage was achieved using complex financial instruments such as off-balance sheet securitization and derivatives, which made it difficult for creditors and regulators to monitor and try to reduce financial institution risk levels.<sup id="cite_ref-papers.ssrn_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers.ssrn-30">[30]</a></sup> These instruments also made it virtually impossible to reorganize financial institutions in bankruptcy, and contributed to the need for government bailouts.<sup id="cite_ref-papers.ssrn_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers.ssrn-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/300px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/450px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png/600px-U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Household_Debt_Relative_to_Disposable_Income_and_GDP.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Household_debt" title="Household debt">Household debt</a> relative to disposable income and GDP.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>US households and financial institutions became increasingly indebted or <a href="/wiki/Financial_leverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial leverage">overleveraged</a> during the years preceding the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-economist1_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist1-126">[126]</a></sup> This increased their vulnerability to the collapse of the housing bubble and worsened the ensuing economic downturn.<sup id="cite_ref-Yellen_on_Minsky_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yellen_on_Minsky-127">[127]</a></sup> Key statistics include:</p>
<p>Free cash used by consumers from home equity extraction doubled from $627&#160;billion in 2001 to $1,428&#160;billion in 2005 as the housing bubble built, a total of nearly $5&#160;trillion over the period, contributing to economic growth worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenspan_Kennedy_Report_-_Table_2_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenspan_Kennedy_Report_-_Table_2-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Equity_extraction_-_Charts_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Equity_extraction_-_Charts-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-130">[130]</a></sup> US home mortgage debt relative to GDP increased from an average of 46% during the 1990s to 73% during 2008, reaching $10.5&#160;trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-money.cnn.com_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-money.cnn.com-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>US household debt as a percentage of annual <a href="/wiki/Disposable_personal_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Disposable personal income">disposable personal income</a> was 127% at the end of 2007, versus 77% in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-economist1_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist1-126">[126]</a></sup> In 1981, US private debt was 123% of GDP; by the third quarter of 2008, it was 290%.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 2004 to 2007, the top five US investment banks each significantly increased their financial leverage (see diagram), which increased their vulnerability to a financial shock. Changes in capital requirements, intended to keep US banks competitive with their European counterparts, allowed lower <i>risk weightings</i> for AAA securities. The shift from first-loss <a href="/wiki/Tranches" class="mw-redirect" title="Tranches">tranches</a> to AAA tranches was seen by regulators as a risk reduction that compensated the higher leverage.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> These five institutions reported over $4.1&#160;trillion in debt for fiscal year 2007, about 30% of US nominal GDP for 2007. <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a> <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_of_Lehman_Brothers" title="Bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers">went bankrupt and was liquidated</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a> were sold at fire-sale prices, and <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a> became commercial banks, subjecting themselves to more stringent regulation. With the exception of Lehman, these companies required or received government support.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Lehman reported that it had been in talks with Bank of America and Barclays for the company's possible sale. However, both Barclays and Bank of America ultimately declined to purchase the entire company.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, two US <a href="/wiki/Government-sponsored_enterprise" title="Government-sponsored enterprise">government-sponsored enterprises</a>, owned or guaranteed nearly $5&#160;trillion in mortgage obligations at the time they were placed into <a href="/wiki/Conservatorship" title="Conservatorship">conservatorship</a> by the US government in September 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-publications1_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-publications1-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>These seven entities were highly leveraged and had $9&#160;trillion in debt or guarantee obligations; yet they were not subject to the same regulation as depository banks.<sup id="cite_ref-Krugman_2009_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krugman_2009-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Behavior that may be optimal for an individual (e.g., saving more during adverse economic conditions) can be detrimental if too many individuals pursue the same behavior, as ultimately one person's consumption is another person's income. Too many consumers attempting to save (or pay down debt) simultaneously is called the <a href="/wiki/Paradox_of_thrift" title="Paradox of thrift">paradox of thrift</a> and can cause or deepen a recession. Economist <a href="/wiki/Hyman_Minsky" title="Hyman Minsky">Hyman Minsky</a> also described a "paradox of deleveraging" as financial institutions that have too much leverage (debt relative to equity) cannot all de-leverage simultaneously without significant declines in the value of their assets.<sup id="cite_ref-Yellen_on_Minsky_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yellen_on_Minsky-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>During April 2009, US Federal Reserve vice-chair <a href="/wiki/Janet_Yellen" title="Janet Yellen">Janet Yellen</a> discussed these paradoxes:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Once this massive credit crunch hit, it didn't take long before we were in a recession. The recession, in turn, deepened the credit crunch as demand and employment fell, and credit losses of financial institutions surged. Indeed, we have been in the grips of precisely this adverse feedback loop for more than a year. A process of balance sheet deleveraging has spread to nearly every corner of the economy. Consumers are pulling back on purchases, especially on durable goods, to build their savings. Businesses are cancelling planned investments and laying off workers to preserve cash.
