And, financial institutions are shrinking assets to bolster capital and improve their chances of weathering the current storm. Once again, Minsky understood this dynamic. He spoke of the paradox of deleveraging, in which precautions that may be smart for individuals and firms—and indeed essential to return the economy to a normal state—nevertheless magnify the distress of the economy as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-Yellen_on_Minsky_127-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yellen_on_Minsky-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_innovation_and_complexity">Financial innovation and complexity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Financial innovation and complexity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png/300px-CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png" width="300" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png/450px-CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png/600px-CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CDO_-_FCIC_and_IMF_Diagram.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
IMF Diagram of CDO and RMBS</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The term <a href="/wiki/Financial_innovation" title="Financial innovation">financial innovation</a> refers to the ongoing development of financial products designed to achieve particular client objectives, such as offsetting a particular risk exposure (such as the default of a borrower) or to assist with obtaining financing. Examples pertinent to this crisis included: the <a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate mortgage</a>; the bundling of subprime mortgages into <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> (MBS) or <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> (CDO) for sale to investors, a type of <a href="/wiki/Securitization" title="Securitization">securitization</a>; and a form of credit insurance called credit default swaps (CDS). The usage of these products expanded dramatically in the years leading up to the crisis. These products vary in complexity and the ease with which they can be valued on the books of financial institutions.</p>
<p>CDO issuance grew from an estimated $20 billion in Q1 2004 to its peak of over $180 billion by Q1 2007, then declined back under $20 billion by Q1 2008. Further, the credit quality of CDO's declined from 2000 to 2007, as the level of subprime and other non-prime mortgage debt increased from 5% to 36% of CDO assets.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> As described in the section on subprime lending, the CDS and portfolio of CDS called <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_CDO" title="Synthetic CDO">synthetic CDO</a> enabled a theoretically infinite amount to be wagered on the finite value of housing loans outstanding, provided that buyers and sellers of the derivatives could be found. For example, buying a CDS to insure a CDO ended up giving the seller the same risk as if they owned the CDO, when those CDO's became worthless.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg/300px-Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg.png" width="300" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg/450px-Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg/600px-Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="769" data-file-height="565" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Complexity_and_the_mortgage_market_-_an_example.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of CMLTI 2006 – NC2</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>This boom in innovative financial products went hand in hand with more complexity. It multiplied the number of actors connected to a single mortgage (including mortgage brokers, specialized originators, the securitizers and their due diligence firms, managing agents and trading desks, and finally investors, insurances and providers of repo funding). With increasing distance from the underlying asset these actors relied more and more on indirect information (including FICO scores on creditworthiness, appraisals and due diligence checks by third party organizations, and most importantly the computer models of rating agencies and risk management desks). Instead of spreading risk this provided the ground for fraudulent acts, misjudgments and finally market collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Wolf" title="Martin Wolf">Martin Wolf</a> further wrote in June 2009 that certain financial innovations enabled firms to circumvent regulations, such as off-balance sheet financing that affects the leverage or capital cushion reported by major banks, stating: "...an enormous part of what banks did in the early part of this decade—the off-balance-sheet vehicles, the derivatives and the 'shadow banking system' itself—was to find a way round regulation."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incorrect_pricing_of_risk">Incorrect pricing of risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Incorrect pricing of risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg/220px-AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg/330px-AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg/440px-AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3340" data-file-height="2504" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AIG_Protester_on_Pine_Street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A protester on <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/AIG_bonus_payments_controversy" title="AIG bonus payments controversy">AIG bonus payments controversy</a> is interviewed by news media.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The pricing of risk refers to the <a href="/wiki/Risk_premium" title="Risk premium">incremental compensation</a> required by investors for taking on additional risk, which may be measured by interest rates or fees. Several scholars have argued that a lack of transparency about banks' risk exposures prevented markets from correctly pricing risk before the crisis, enabled the mortgage market to grow larger than it otherwise would have, and made the financial crisis far more disruptive than it would have been if risk levels had been disclosed in a straightforward, readily understandable format.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-papers.ssrn_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers.ssrn-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>For a variety of reasons, market participants did not accurately measure the risk inherent with financial innovation such as MBS and CDOs or understand its effect on the overall stability of the financial system.<sup id="cite_ref-Declaration_of_G20_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Declaration_of_G20-39">[39]</a></sup> For example, the pricing model for CDOs clearly did not reflect the level of risk they introduced into the system. Banks estimated that $450bn of CDO were sold between "late 2005 to the middle of 2007"; among the $102bn of those that had been liquidated, JPMorgan estimated that the average recovery rate for "high quality" CDOs was approximately 32&#160;cents on the dollar, while the recovery rate for <a href="/wiki/Mezzanine_capital" title="Mezzanine capital">mezzanine</a> CDO was approximately five cents for every dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-TPMBLOG1_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TPMBLOG1-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another example relates to <a href="/wiki/AIG" class="mw-redirect" title="AIG">AIG</a>, which insured obligations of various financial institutions through the usage of credit default swaps. The basic CDS transaction involved AIG receiving a premium in exchange for a promise to pay money to party A in the event party B defaulted. However, AIG did not have the financial strength to support its many CDS commitments as the crisis progressed and was taken over by the government in September 2008. US taxpayers provided over $180&#160;billion in government support to AIG during 2008 and early 2009, through which the money flowed to various counterparties to CDS transactions, including many large global financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> (FCIC) made the major government study of the crisis. It concluded in January 2011:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The Commission concludes AIG failed and was rescued by the government primarily because its enormous sales of credit default swaps were made without putting up the initial collateral, setting aside capital reserves, or hedging its exposure – a profound failure in corporate governance, particularly its risk management practices. AIG's failure was possible because of the sweeping deregulation of over-the-counter (OTC) derivatives, including credit default swaps, which effectively eliminated federal and state regulation of these products, including capital and margin requirements that would have lessened the likelihood of AIG's failure.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The limitations of a widely used financial model also were not properly understood.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Felix1_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Felix1-150">[150]</a></sup> This formula assumed that the price of CDS was correlated with and could predict the correct price of mortgage-backed securities. Because it was highly tractable, it rapidly came to be used by a huge percentage of CDO and CDS investors, issuers, and rating agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-Felix1_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Felix1-150">[150]</a></sup> According to one wired.com article:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Then the model fell apart. Cracks started appearing early on, when financial markets began behaving in ways that users of <a href="/wiki/David_X._Li" title="David X. Li">Li's</a> formula hadn't expected. The cracks became full-fledged canyons in 2008—when ruptures in the financial system's foundation swallowed up trillions of dollars and put the survival of the global banking system in serious peril... Li's <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_copula" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaussian copula">Gaussian copula</a> formula will go down in history as instrumental in causing the unfathomable losses that brought the world financial system to its knees.<sup id="cite_ref-Felix1_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Felix1-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>As financial assets became more complex and harder to value, investors were reassured by the fact that the international <a href="/wiki/Bond_rating" class="mw-redirect" title="Bond rating">bond rating</a> agencies and bank regulators accepted as valid some complex mathematical models that showed the risks were much smaller than they actually were.<sup id="cite_ref-RISKS1_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RISKS1-151">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Soros" title="George Soros">George Soros</a> commented that "The super-boom got out of hand when the new products became so complicated that the authorities could no longer calculate the risks and started relying on the risk management methods of the banks themselves. Similarly, the rating agencies relied on the information provided by the originators of synthetic products. It was a shocking abdication of responsibility."<sup id="cite_ref-WORSTCRISIS_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WORSTCRISIS-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moreover, a conflict of interest between <a href="/wiki/Investment_management" title="Investment management">professional investment managers</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Institutional_investor" title="Institutional investor">institutional clients</a>, combined with a global glut in investment capital, led to bad investments by asset managers in over-priced credit assets. Professional investment managers generally are compensated based on the volume of client <a href="/wiki/Assets_under_management" title="Assets under management">assets under management</a>. There is, therefore, an incentive for asset managers to expand their assets under management in order to maximize their compensation. As the glut in global investment capital caused the yields on credit assets to decline, asset managers were faced with the choice of either investing in assets where returns did not reflect true credit risk or returning funds to clients. Many asset managers continued to invest client funds in over-priced (under-yielding) investments, to the detriment of their clients, so they could maintain their assets under management. They supported this choice with a "plausible deniability" of the risks associated with subprime-based credit assets because the loss experience with early "vintages" of subprime loans was so low.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the dominance of the above formula, there are documented attempts of the financial industry, occurring before the crisis, to address the formula limitations, specifically the lack of dependence dynamics and the poor representation of extreme events.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The volume "Credit Correlation: Life After Copulas", published in 2007 by World Scientific, summarizes a 2006 conference held by Merrill Lynch in London where several practitioners attempted to propose models rectifying some of the copula limitations. See also the article by Donnelly and Embrechts<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and the book by Brigo, Pallavicini and Torresetti, that reports relevant warnings and research on CDOs appeared in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mortgage risks were underestimated by every institution in the chain from originator to investor by underweighting the possibility of falling housing prices based on historical trends of the past 50 years. Limitations of default and prepayment models, the heart of pricing models, led to overvaluation of mortgage and asset-backed products and their derivatives by originators, securitizers, broker-dealers, rating-agencies, insurance underwriters and investors. <sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boom_and_collapse_of_the_shadow_banking_system">Boom and collapse of the shadow banking system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Boom and collapse of the shadow banking system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Securitization_Market_Activity.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/350px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/525px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Securitization_Market_Activity.png/700px-Securitization_Market_Activity.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Securitization_Market_Activity.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Securitization markets were impaired during the crisis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is strong evidence that the riskiest, worst performing mortgages were funded through the "shadow banking system" and that competition from the shadow banking system may have pressured more traditional institutions to lower their own underwriting standards and originate riskier loans.<sup id="cite_ref-ssrn_28-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ssrn-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a June 2008 speech, President and CEO of the New York Federal Reserve Bank <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Geithner" title="Timothy Geithner">Timothy Geithner</a>—who in 2009 became Secretary of the United States Treasury—placed significant blame for the freezing of credit markets on a "run" on the entities in the "parallel" banking system, also called the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a>. These entities became critical to the credit markets underpinning the financial system, but were not subject to the same regulatory controls. Further, these entities were vulnerable because of <a href="/wiki/Asset_liability_mismatch" class="mw-redirect" title="Asset liability mismatch">maturity mismatch</a>, meaning that they borrowed short-term in liquid markets to purchase long-term, illiquid and risky assets. This meant that disruptions in credit markets would make them subject to rapid deleveraging, selling their long-term assets at depressed prices. He described the significance of these entities:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In early 2007, asset-backed commercial paper conduits, in structured investment vehicles, in auction-rate preferred securities, tender option bonds and variable rate demand notes, had a combined asset size of roughly $2.2&#160;trillion. Assets financed overnight in triparty repo grew to $2.5&#160;trillion. Assets held in hedge funds grew to roughly $1.8&#160;trillion. The combined balance sheets of the then five major investment banks totaled $4&#160;trillion. In comparison, the total assets of the top five bank holding companies in the United States at that point were just over $6&#160;trillion, and total assets of the entire banking system were about $10&#160;trillion. The combined effect of these factors was a financial system vulnerable to self-reinforcing asset price and credit cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-newyorkfed.org_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorkfed.org-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a>, laureate of the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Prize in Economics</a>, described the run on the shadow banking system as the "core of what happened" to cause the crisis. He referred to this lack of controls as "malign neglect" and argued that regulation should have been imposed on all banking-like activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Krugman_2009_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krugman_2009-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>The securitization markets supported by the shadow banking system started to close down in the spring of 2007 and nearly shut-down in the fall of 2008. More than a third of the private credit markets thus became unavailable as a source of funds.<sup id="cite_ref-Search_Site_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Search_Site-159">[159]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, the traditional banking system does not have the capital to close this gap as of June 2009: "It would take a number of years of strong profits to generate sufficient capital to support that additional lending volume." The authors also indicate that some forms of securitization are "likely to vanish forever, having been an artifact of excessively loose credit conditions."<sup id="cite_ref-brookings.edu_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brookings.edu-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commodities_boom">Commodities boom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Commodities boom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a></div>
<p>Rapid increases in a number of commodity prices followed the collapse in the housing bubble. The price of oil nearly tripled from $50 to $147 from early 2007 to 2008, before plunging as the financial crisis began to take hold in late 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Experts debate the causes, with some attributing it to speculative flow of money from housing and other investments into commodities, some to monetary policy,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> and some to the increasing feeling of raw materials scarcity in a fast-growing world, leading to long positions taken on those markets, such as Chinese increasing presence in Africa. An increase in oil prices tends to divert a larger share of consumer spending into gasoline, which creates downward pressure on economic growth in oil importing countries, as wealth flows to oil-producing states.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> A pattern of spiking instability in the price of oil over the decade leading up to the price high of 2008 has been recently identified.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The destabilizing effects of this price variance has been proposed as a contributory factor in the financial crisis.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Copper_Price_History_USD.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/350px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png" width="350" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/525px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Copper_Price_History_USD.png/700px-Copper_Price_History_USD.png 2x" data-file-width="934" data-file-height="402" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Copper_Price_History_USD.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global copper prices</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Copper prices increased at the same time as oil prices. Copper traded at about $2,500 per ton from 1990 until 1999, when it fell to about $1,600. The price slump lasted until 2004, when a price surge pushed copper to $7,040 per ton in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nickel prices boomed in the late 1990s, then declined from around $51,000 /£36,700 per metric ton in May 2007 to about $11,550/£8,300 per metric ton in January 2009. Prices were only just starting to recover as of January 2010, but most of Australia's nickel mines had gone bankrupt by then.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> As the price for high grade <a href="/wiki/Nickel_sulphate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel sulphate">nickel sulphate</a> ore recovered in 2010, so did the Australian nickel mining industry.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coincidentally with these price fluctuations, long-only commodity index funds became popular—by one estimate investment increased from $90&#160;billion in 2006 to $200&#160;billion at the end of 2007, while commodity prices increased 71% – which raised concern as to whether these index funds caused the commodity bubble.<sup id="cite_ref-IrwinSanders2010_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrwinSanders2010-168">[168]</a></sup> The empirical research has been mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-IrwinSanders2010_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IrwinSanders2010-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systemic_crisis">Systemic crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Systemic crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another analysis, different from the <a href="/wiki/Mainstream" title="Mainstream">mainstream</a> explanation, is that the financial crisis is merely a symptom of another, deeper crisis, which is a systemic crisis of capitalism itself.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ravi_Batra" title="Ravi Batra">Ravi Batra</a>'s theory is that growing inequality of <a href="/wiki/Financial_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial capitalism">financial capitalism</a> produces speculative bubbles that burst and result in depression and major <a href="/wiki/The_Downfall_of_Capitalism_and_Communism" title="The Downfall of Capitalism and Communism">political changes</a>. He has also suggested that a "demand gap" related to differing wage and productivity growth explains deficit and debt dynamics important to stock market developments.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Bellamy_Foster" title="John Bellamy Foster">John Bellamy Foster</a>, a political economy analyst and editor of the <a href="/wiki/Monthly_Review" title="Monthly Review">Monthly Review</a>, believes that the decrease in GDP growth rates since the early 1970s is due to increasing <a href="/wiki/Market_saturation" title="Market saturation">market saturation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2005, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Bogle" title="John C. Bogle">John C. Bogle</a> wrote that a series of challenges face capitalism that have contributed to past financial crises and have not been sufficiently addressed:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Corporate America went astray largely because the power of managers went virtually unchecked by our gatekeepers for far too long... They failed to 'keep an eye on these geniuses' to whom they had entrusted the responsibility of the management of America's great corporations.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Echoing the central thesis of <a href="/wiki/James_Burnham" title="James Burnham">James Burnham's</a> 1941 seminal book, <a href="/wiki/James_Burnham#The_Managerial_Revolution" title="James Burnham">The Managerial Revolution</a>, Bogle cites particular issues, including:<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>that "Manager's capitalism" has replaced "owner's capitalism", meaning management runs the firm for its benefit rather than for the shareholders, a variation on the <a href="/wiki/Principal%E2%80%93agent_problem" title="Principal–agent problem">principal–agent problem</a>;</li>
<li>the burgeoning executive compensation;</li>
<li>the management of earnings, mainly a focus on share price rather than the creation of genuine value; and</li>
<li>the failure of gatekeepers, including auditors, boards of directors, Wall Street analysts, and career politicians.</li>
</ul>
<p>An analysis conducted by <a href="/wiki/Mark_Roeder" title="Mark Roeder">Mark Roeder</a>, a former executive at the Swiss-based <a href="/wiki/UBS" title="UBS">UBS</a> Bank, suggested that large-scale momentum, or <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Mo" title="The Big Mo">The Big Mo</a> "played a pivotal role" in the 2008–09 global financial crisis. Roeder suggested that "recent technological advances, such as computer-driven trading programs, together with the increasingly interconnected nature of markets, has magnified the momentum effect. This has made the financial sector inherently unstable."<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Reich" title="Robert Reich">Robert Reich</a> attributes the current economic downturn to the stagnation of wages in the United States, particularly those of the hourly workers who comprise 80% of the workforce. He says this stagnation forced the population to borrow to meet the cost of living.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>Economists <a href="/wiki/Ailsa_McKay" title="Ailsa McKay">Ailsa McKay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margunn_Bj%C3%B8rnholt" title="Margunn Bjørnholt">Margunn Bjørnholt</a> argue that the financial crisis and the response to it revealed a crisis of ideas in mainstream economics and within the economics profession, and call for a reshaping of both the economy, economic theory and the economics profession.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_economic_forecasting">Role of economic forecasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Role of economic forecasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The former Governor of the <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Bank_of_India" title="Reserve Bank of India">Reserve Bank of India</a> <a href="/wiki/Raghuram_Rajan" title="Raghuram Rajan">Raghuram Rajan</a> had predicted the crisis in 2005 when he became chief economist at the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.In 2005, at a celebration honouring <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a>, who was about to retire as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/US_Federal_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="US Federal Reserve">US Federal Reserve</a>, Rajan delivered a controversial paper that was critical of the financial sector.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In that paper, "Has Financial Development Made the World Riskier?", Rajan "argued that disaster might loom."<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-177">[177]</a></sup> Rajan argued that financial sector managers were encouraged to "take risks that generate severe adverse consequences with small probability but, in return, offer generous compensation the rest of the time. These risks are known as tail risks. But perhaps the most important concern is whether banks will be able to provide liquidity to financial markets so that if the tail risk does materialise, financial positions can be unwound and losses allocated so that the consequences to the real economy are minimised."</p>
<p>The financial crisis was not widely predicted by <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_economics" title="Mainstream economics">mainstream economists</a> except <a href="/wiki/Raghuram_Rajan" title="Raghuram Rajan">Raghuram Rajan</a>, who instead spoke of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Moderation" title="Great Moderation">Great Moderation</a>. A number of <a href="/wiki/Heterodox_economics" title="Heterodox economics">heterodox economists</a> predicted the crisis, with varying arguments. Dirk Bezemer in his research<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> credits (with supporting argument and estimates of timing) 12 economists with predicting the crisis: <a href="/wiki/Dean_Baker" title="Dean Baker">Dean Baker</a> (US), <a href="/wiki/Wynne_Godley" title="Wynne Godley">Wynne Godley</a> (UK), <a href="/wiki/Fred_Harrison_(author)" title="Fred Harrison (author)">Fred Harrison</a> (UK), <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hudson_(economist)" title="Michael Hudson (economist)">Michael Hudson</a> (US), <a href="/wiki/Eric_Janszen" title="Eric Janszen">Eric Janszen</a> (US), <a href="/wiki/Steve_Keen" title="Steve Keen">Steve Keen</a> (Australia), <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Broechner_Madsen" class="mw-redirect" title="Jakob Broechner Madsen">Jakob Brøchner Madsen</a> &amp; Jens Kjaer Sørensen (Denmark), <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Richeb%C3%A4cher" title="Kurt Richebächer">Kurt Richebächer</a> (US), <a href="/wiki/Nouriel_Roubini" title="Nouriel Roubini">Nouriel Roubini</a> (US), <a href="/wiki/Peter_Schiff" title="Peter Schiff">Peter Schiff</a> (US), and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shiller" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Shiller">Robert Shiller</a> (US). Examples of other experts who gave indications of a financial crisis have also been given.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Not surprisingly, the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Austrian school">Austrian economic school</a> regarded the crisis as a vindication and classic example of a predictable credit-fueled bubble that could not forestall the disregarded but inevitable effect of an artificial, manufactured laxity in monetary supply,<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> a perspective that even former Fed Chair Alan Greenspan in Congressional testimony confessed himself forced to return to.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>A cover story in <i><a href="/wiki/BusinessWeek" class="mw-redirect" title="BusinessWeek">BusinessWeek</a></i> magazine claims that economists mostly failed to predict the worst international economic crisis since the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania</a>'s online business journal examines why economists failed to predict a major global financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Popular articles published in the mass media have led the general public to believe that the majority of economists have failed in their obligation to predict the financial crisis. For example, an article in the New York Times informs that economist <a href="/wiki/Nouriel_Roubini" title="Nouriel Roubini">Nouriel Roubini</a> warned of such crisis as early as September 2006, and the article goes on to state that the profession of economics is bad at predicting recessions.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, Roubini was ridiculed for predicting a collapse of the housing market and worldwide recession, while <i>The New York Times</i> labelled him "Dr. Doom".<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shiller, an expert in <a href="/wiki/Case-Shiller_index" class="mw-redirect" title="Case-Shiller index">housing markets</a>, wrote an article a year before the collapse of Lehman Brothers in which he predicted that a slowing US housing market would cause the housing bubble to burst, leading to financial collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Schiff regularly appeared on television in the years before the crisis and warned of the impending real estate collapse.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within mainstream <a href="/wiki/Financial_economics" title="Financial economics">financial economics</a>, most believe that financial crises are simply unpredictable,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> following Eugene Fama's <a href="/wiki/Efficient-market_hypothesis" title="Efficient-market hypothesis">efficient-market hypothesis</a> and the related <a href="/wiki/Random_walk_hypothesis" title="Random walk hypothesis">random-walk hypothesis</a>, which state respectively that markets contain all information about possible future movements, and that the movements of financial prices are random and unpredictable. Recent research casts doubt on the accuracy of "early warning" systems of potential crises, which must also predict their timing.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stock trader and financial risk engineer <a href="/wiki/Nassim_Nicholas_Taleb" title="Nassim Nicholas Taleb">Nassim Nicholas Taleb</a>, author of the 2007 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Swan_(Taleb_book)" title="The Black Swan (Taleb book)">The Black Swan</a></i>, spent years warning against the breakdown of the banking system in particular and the economy in general owing to their use of bad risk models and reliance on forecasting, and their reliance on bad models, and framed the problem as part of "robustness and fragility".<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> He also took action against the establishment view by making a big financial bet on banking stocks and making a fortune from the crisis ("They didn't listen, so I took their money").<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> According to David Brooks from the New York Times, "Taleb not only has an explanation for what’s happening, he saw it coming."<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_financial_markets">Impact on financial markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Impact on financial markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_stock_market">US stock market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: US stock market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_bear_market_of_2007%E2%80%932009" class="mw-redirect" title="United States bear market of 2007–2009">United States bear market of 2007–2009</a></div>
<p>The US stock market peaked in October 2007, when the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> index exceeded 14,000 points. It then entered a pronounced decline, which accelerated markedly in October 2008. By March 2009, the Dow Jones average had reached a trough of around 6,600. Four years later, it hit an all-time high. It is probable, but debated, that the Federal Reserve's aggressive policy of <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_easing" title="Quantitative easing">quantitative easing</a> spurred the partial recovery in the stock market.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>Market strategist Phil Dow believes distinctions exist "between the current market malaise" and the Great Depression. He says the Dow Jones average's fall of more than 50% over a period of 17 months is similar to a 54.7% fall in the Great Depression, followed by a total drop of 89% over the following 16 months. "It's very troubling if you have a mirror image," said Dow.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Norris" title="Floyd Norris">Floyd Norris</a>, the chief financial correspondent of <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a>, wrote in a blog entry in March 2009 that the decline has not been a mirror image of the Great Depression, explaining that although the decline amounts were nearly the same at the time, the rates of decline had started much faster in 2007, and that the past year had only ranked eighth among the worst recorded years of percentage drops in the Dow. The past two years ranked third, however.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_institutions">Financial institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Financial institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation_of_Northern_Rock" title="Nationalisation of Northern Rock">Nationalisation of Northern Rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Federal_takeover_of_Fannie_Mae_and_Freddie_Mac" title="Federal takeover of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac">Federal takeover of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg/220px-Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg/330px-Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg/440px-Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Rock_Queue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Northern_Rock#Subprime_mortgage_crisis_and_nationalisation" title="Northern Rock">2007 bank run on Northern Rock</a>, a UK bank</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first notable event signaling a possible financial crisis occurred in the United Kingdom on August 9, 2007, when <a href="/wiki/BNP_Paribas" title="BNP Paribas">BNP Paribas</a>, citing "a complete evaporation of liquidity", blocked withdrawals from three hedge funds. The significance of this event was not immediately recognized but soon led to a panic as investors and savers attempted to liquidate assets deposited in highly leveraged financial institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-G8512_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-G8512-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The International Monetary Fund estimated that large US and European banks lost more than $1&#160;trillion on toxic assets and from bad loans from January 2007 to September 2009. These losses are expected to top $2.8&#160;trillion from 2007 to 2010. US bank losses were forecast to hit $1&#160;trillion and European bank losses will reach $1.6&#160;trillion. The <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF) estimated that US banks were about 60% through their losses, but British and eurozone banks only 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the first victims was <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rock" title="Northern Rock">Northern Rock</a>, a medium-sized British bank.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The highly <a href="/wiki/Leverage_(finance)" title="Leverage (finance)">leveraged</a> nature of its business led the bank to request security from the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>. This in turn led to investor panic and a <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank run</a><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> in mid-September 2007. Calls by Liberal Democrat Treasury Spokesman <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Cable" class="mw-redirect" title="Vincent Cable">Vince Cable</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nationalised" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalised">nationalise</a> the institution were initially ignored; in February 2008, however, the <a href="/wiki/British_government" class="mw-redirect" title="British government">British government</a> (having failed to find a private sector buyer) relented, and the bank was taken into public hands. <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation_of_Northern_Rock" title="Nationalisation of Northern Rock">Northern Rock's problems</a> proved to be an early indication of the troubles that would soon befall other banks and financial institutions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="IndyMac">IndyMac</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: IndyMac">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first visible institution to run into trouble in the United States was the Southern California–based <a href="/wiki/IndyMac" title="IndyMac">IndyMac</a>, a spin-off of <a href="/wiki/Countrywide_Financial" class="mw-redirect" title="Countrywide Financial">Countrywide Financial</a>. Before its failure, IndyMac Bank was the largest <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_association" title="Savings and loan association">savings and loan association</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> market and the seventh largest <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_loan" title="Mortgage loan">mortgage</a> originator in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> The failure of IndyMac Bank on July 11, 2008, was the fourth largest <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank failure</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> history up until the crisis precipitated even larger failures,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> and the second largest failure of a regulated <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_association" title="Savings and loan association">thrift</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Veiga_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veiga-206">[206]</a></sup> IndyMac Bank's parent corporation was IndyMac Bancorp until the FDIC seized IndyMac Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-LaCapra_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaCapra-207">[207]</a></sup> IndyMac Bancorp filed for <a href="/wiki/Chapter_7,_Title_11,_United_States_Code" title="Chapter 7, Title 11, United States Code">Chapter 7 bankruptcy</a> in July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-LaCapra_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaCapra-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>IndyMac Bank was founded as Countrywide Mortgage Investment in 1985 by <a href="/wiki/David_S._Loeb" title="David S. Loeb">David S. Loeb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Mozilo" title="Angelo Mozilo">Angelo Mozilo</a><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> as a means of <a href="/wiki/Cross-collateralization" title="Cross-collateralization">collateralizing</a> <a href="/wiki/Countrywide_Financial" class="mw-redirect" title="Countrywide Financial">Countrywide Financial</a> loans too big to be sold to Freddie Mac and Fannie Mae. In 1997, Countrywide spun off IndyMac as an independent company run by Mike Perry, who remained its CEO until the downfall of the bank in July 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Paletta_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paletta-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p>The primary causes of its failure were largely associated with its business strategy of originating and securitizing <a href="/wiki/Alt-A" title="Alt-A">Alt-A</a> loans on a large scale. This strategy resulted in rapid growth and a high concentration of risky assets. From its inception as a savings association in 2000, IndyMac grew to the seventh largest savings and loan and ninth largest originator of mortgage loans in the United States. During 2006, IndyMac originated over $90 billion of mortgages.</p>
<p>IndyMac’s aggressive growth strategy, use of Alt-A and other nontraditional loan products, insufficient underwriting, credit concentrations in residential real estate in the <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a> markets—states, alongside Nevada and Arizona, where the <a href="/wiki/United_States_housing_bubble" title="United States housing bubble">housing bubble</a> was most pronounced—and heavy reliance on costly funds borrowed from a <a href="/wiki/Federal_Home_Loan_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Home Loan Bank">Federal Home Loan Bank</a> (FHLB) and from brokered deposits, led to its demise when the mortgage market declined in 2007.</p>
<p>IndyMac often made loans without verification of the borrower’s income or assets, and to borrowers with poor credit histories. Appraisals obtained by IndyMac on underlying collateral were often questionable as well. As an Alt-A lender, IndyMac’s business model was to offer loan products to fit the borrower’s needs, using an extensive array of risky option-<a href="/wiki/Adjustable-rate_mortgage" title="Adjustable-rate mortgage">adjustable-rate-mortgages</a> (option ARMs), subprime loans, 80/20 loans, and other nontraditional products. Ultimately, loans were made to many borrowers who simply could not afford to make their payments. The thrift remained profitable only as long as it was able to sell those loans in the <a href="/wiki/Secondary_mortgage_market" title="Secondary mortgage market">secondary mortgage market</a>. IndyMac resisted efforts to regulate its involvement in those loans or tighten their issuing criteria: see the comment by Ruthann Melbourne, Chief Risk Officer, to the regulating agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-ustreas.gov_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ustreas.gov-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<p>May 12, 2008, in a small note in the "Capital" section of its what would become its last <a href="/wiki/10-Q" class="mw-redirect" title="10-Q">10-Q</a> released before receivership, IndyMac revealed – but did not admit – that it was no longer a well-capitalized institution and that it was headed for insolvency.</p>
<p>IndyMac reported that during April 2008, <a href="/wiki/Moody%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Moody's">Moody's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor's">Standard &amp; Poor's</a> downgraded the ratings on a significant number of <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">Mortgage-backed security</a> (MBS) bonds—including $160 million issued by IndyMac that the bank retained in its MBS portfolio. IndyMac concluded that these downgrades would have harmed the Company's risk-based capital ratio as of June 30, 2008. Had these lowered ratings been in effect at March 31, 2008, IndyMac concluded that the bank's capital ratio would have been 9.27% total risk-based. IndyMac warned that if its regulators found its capital position to have fallen below "well capitalized" (minimum 10% risk-based capital ratio) to "adequately capitalized" (8–10% risk-based capital ratio) the bank might no longer be able to use brokered deposits as a source of funds.</p>
<p>Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Schumer" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Schumer">Charles Schumer</a> (D-NY) later pointed out that brokered deposits made up more than 37 percent of IndyMac's total deposits, and ask the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a> (FDIC) whether it had considered ordering IndyMac to reduce its reliance on these deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> With $18.9 billion in total deposits reported on March 31,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Senator Schumer would have been referring to a little over $7 billion in brokered deposits. While the breakout of maturities of these deposits is not known exactly, a simple averaging would have put the threat of brokered deposits loss to IndyMac at $500 million a month, had the regulator disallowed IndyMac from acquiring new brokered deposits on June 30.</p>
<p>IndyMac was taking new measures to preserve capital, such as deferring interest payments on some preferred securities. Dividends on common shares had already been suspended for the first quarter of 2008, after being cut in half the previous quarter. The company still had not secured a significant capital infusion nor found a ready buyer.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<p>IndyMac reported that the bank's risk-based capital was only $47 million above the minimum required for this 10% mark. But it did not reveal some of that $47 million capital it claimed it had, as of March 31, 2008, was fabricated.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse">Collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikinews-logo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" width="40" height="22" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="759" data-file-height="415" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news: <i><b><a href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/Category:July_15,_2008#IndyMac_Bank_placed_into_conservatorship_by_US_Government" class="extiw" title="wikinews:Category:July 15, 2008">IndyMac Bank placed into conservatorship by US Government</a></b></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>When home prices declined in the latter half of 2007 and the secondary mortgage market collapsed, IndyMac was forced to hold $10.7 billion of loans it could not sell in the secondary market. Its reduced liquidity was further exacerbated in late June 2008 when account holders withdrew $1.55 billion or about 7.5% of IndyMac's deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-secinfo.com_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-secinfo.com-218">[218]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">“run”</a> on the thrift followed the public release of a letter from Senator Charles Schumer to the FDIC and OTS. The letter outlined the Senator’s concerns with IndyMac. While the run was a contributing factor in the timing of IndyMac’s demise, the underlying cause of the failure was the unsafe and unsound way they operated the thrift.<sup id="cite_ref-ustreas.gov_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ustreas.gov-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 26, 2008, Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_Schumer" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Schumer">Charles Schumer</a> (D-NY), a member of the <a href="/wiki/Senate_Banking_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Banking Committee">Senate Banking Committee</a>, chairman of Congress' <a href="/wiki/Joint_Economic_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Joint Economic Committee">Joint Economic Committee</a> and the third-ranking Democrat in the Senate,<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> released several letters he had sent to regulators, which warned that, "The possible collapse of big mortgage lender IndyMac Bancorp Inc. poses significant financial risks to its borrowers and depositors, and regulators may not be ready to intervene to protect them." Some worried depositors began to withdraw money.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 7, 2008, IndyMac announced on the company blog that it:</p>
<ul>
<li>Had failed to raise <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> since its May 12, 2008 quarterly earnings report;</li>
<li>Had been notified by bank and thrift regulators that IndyMac Bank was no longer deemed "well-capitalized";</li>
</ul>
<p>IndyMac announced the closure of both its retail lending and wholesale divisions, halted new loan submissions, and cut 3,800 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 11, 2008, citing liquidity concerns, the FDIC put IndyMac Bank into <a href="/wiki/Conservatorship" title="Conservatorship">conservatorship</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Bridge_bank_(United_States)" title="Bridge bank (United States)">bridge bank</a>, IndyMac Federal Bank, FSB, was established to assume control of IndyMac Bank's assets, its secured liabilities, and its insured deposit accounts. The FDIC announced plans to open IndyMac Federal Bank, FSB on July 14, 2008. Until then, depositors would have access their insured deposits through ATMs, their existing checks, and their existing debit cards. Telephone and Internet account access was restored when the bank reopened.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt4_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt4-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-closeletter_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-closeletter-223">[223]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fn1_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn1-224">[224]</a></sup> The FDIC guarantees the funds of all insured accounts up to US$100,000, and has declared a special advance dividend to the roughly 10,000 depositors with funds in excess of the insured amount, guaranteeing 50% of any amounts in excess of $100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Veiga_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Veiga-206">[206]</a></sup> Yet, even with the pending sale of Indymac to IMB Management Holdings, an estimated 10,000 uninsured depositors of Indymac are still at a loss of over $270 million.<sup id="cite_ref-articles.latimes.com_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-articles.latimes.com-225">[225]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<p>With $32 billion in assets, IndyMac Bank was one of the largest bank failures in American history.</p>
<p>IndyMac Bancorp filed for <a href="/wiki/Chapter_7,_Title_11,_United_States_Code" title="Chapter 7, Title 11, United States Code">Chapter 7 bankruptcy</a> on July 31, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-LaCapra_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LaCapra-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Initially the companies affected were those directly involved in home construction and mortgage lending such as Northern Rock and <a href="/wiki/Countrywide_Financial" class="mw-redirect" title="Countrywide Financial">Countrywide Financial</a>, as they could no longer obtain financing through the credit markets. Over 100 mortgage lenders went bankrupt during 2007 and 2008. Concerns that investment bank <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> would collapse in March 2008 resulted in its fire-sale to <a href="/wiki/JP_Morgan_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="JP Morgan Chase">JP Morgan Chase</a>. The financial institution crisis hit its peak in September and October 2008. Several major institutions either failed, were acquired under duress, or were subject to government takeover. These included <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>, Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac, <a href="/wiki/Washington_Mutual" title="Washington Mutual">Washington Mutual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wachovia" title="Wachovia">Wachovia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a>, and <a href="/wiki/AIG" class="mw-redirect" title="AIG">AIG</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-foreignaffairs1_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreignaffairs1-227">[227]</a></sup> On Oct. 6, 2008, three weeks after Lehman Brothers filed the largest bankruptcy in US history, Lehman's former CEO found himself before Representative Henry A. Waxman, the California Democrat who chaired the House Committee on Oversight and Government Reform. <a href="/wiki/Richard_S._Fuld,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Richard S. Fuld, Jr.">Fuld</a> said he was a victim of the collapse, blaming a "crisis of confidence" in the markets for dooming his firm.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Credit_markets_and_the_shadow_banking_system">Credit markets and the shadow banking system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Credit markets and the shadow banking system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TED_spread_2008.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TED_spread_2008.svg/350px-TED_spread_2008.svg.png" width="350" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TED_spread_2008.svg/525px-TED_spread_2008.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TED_spread_2008.svg/700px-TED_spread_2008.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TED_spread_2008.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
TED spread and components during 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 2008, the crisis hit its most critical stage. There was the equivalent of a <a href="/wiki/Bank_run" title="Bank run">bank run</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Money_market_fund" title="Money market fund">money market funds</a>, which frequently invest in <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">commercial paper</a> issued by corporations to fund their operations and payrolls. Withdrawal from money markets were $144.5&#160;billion during one week, versus $7.1&#160;billion the week prior. This interrupted the ability of corporations to rollover (replace) their <a href="/wiki/Money_market" title="Money market">short-term debt</a>. The US government responded by extending insurance for money market accounts analogous to bank <a href="/wiki/Deposit_insurance" title="Deposit insurance">deposit insurance</a> via a temporary guarantee<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> and with Federal Reserve programs to purchase commercial paper. The <a href="/wiki/TED_spread" title="TED spread">TED spread</a>, an indicator of perceived credit risk in the general economy, spiked up in July 2007, remained volatile for a year, then spiked even higher in September 2008,<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> reaching a record 4.65% on October 10, 2008.</p>
<p>In a dramatic meeting on September 18, 2008, Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Henry_Paulson" title="Henry Paulson">Henry Paulson</a> and Fed chairman <a href="/wiki/Ben_Bernanke" title="Ben Bernanke">Ben Bernanke</a> met with key legislators to propose a $700&#160;billion emergency bailout. Bernanke reportedly told them: "If we don't do this, we may not have an economy on Monday."<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Economic_Stabilization_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Emergency Economic Stabilization Act">Emergency Economic Stabilization Act</a>, which implemented the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP), was signed into law on October 3, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Associated_Press-Raum-2008-10-03_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Associated_Press-Raum-2008-10-03-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Economist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a> and US Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Geithner" title="Timothy Geithner">Timothy Geithner</a> explain the credit crisis via the implosion of the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a>, which had grown to nearly equal the importance of the traditional commercial banking sector as described above. Without the ability to obtain investor funds in exchange for most types of <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asset-backed_commercial_paper" title="Asset-backed commercial paper">asset-backed commercial paper</a>, investment banks and other entities in the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Shadow banking">shadow banking</a> system could not provide funds to mortgage firms and other corporations.<sup id="cite_ref-newyorkfed.org_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newyorkfed.org-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Krugman_2009_117-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krugman_2009-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>This meant that nearly one-third of the US lending mechanism was frozen and continued to be frozen into June 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Search_Site_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Search_Site-159">[159]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, the traditional banking system does not have the capital to close this gap as of June 2009: "It would take a number of years of strong profits to generate sufficient capital to support that additional lending volume". The authors also indicate that some forms of <a href="/wiki/Securitization" title="Securitization">securitization</a> are "likely to vanish forever, having been an artifact of excessively loose credit conditions". While traditional banks have raised their lending standards, it was the collapse of the shadow banking system that is the primary cause of the reduction in funds available for borrowing.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wealth_effects">Wealth effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Wealth effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/170px-Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/255px-Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg/340px-Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lehman_Brothers_Times_Square_by_David_Shankbone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The New York City headquarters of Lehman Brothers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is a direct relationship between declines in wealth and declines in consumption and business investment, which along with government spending, represent the economic engine. Between June 2007 and November 2008, Americans lost an estimated average of more than a quarter of their collective net worth.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By early November 2008, a broad US stock index the S&amp;P 500, was down 45% from its 2007 high. Housing prices had dropped 20% from their 2006 peak, with futures markets signaling a 30–35% potential drop. Total home equity in the United States, which was valued at $13&#160;trillion at its peak in 2006, had dropped to $8.8&#160;trillion by mid-2008 and was still falling in late 2008. Total retirement assets, Americans' second-largest household asset, dropped by 22%, from $10.3&#160;trillion in 2006 to $8&#160;trillion in mid-2008. During the same period, savings and investment assets (apart from retirement savings) lost $1.2&#160;trillion and pension assets lost $1.3&#160;trillion. Taken together, these losses total a staggering $8.3&#160;trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-foreignaffairs1_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreignaffairs1-227">[227]</a></sup> Since peaking in the second quarter of 2007, household wealth is down&#160;$14&#160;trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>Further, US homeowners had extracted significant equity in their homes in the years leading up to the crisis, which they could no longer do once housing prices collapsed. Free cash used by consumers from home equity extraction doubled from $627&#160;billion in 2001 to $1,428&#160;billion in 2005 as the housing bubble built, a total of nearly $5&#160;trillion over the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenspan_Kennedy_Report_-_Table_2_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenspan_Kennedy_Report_-_Table_2-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Equity_extraction_-_Charts_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Equity_extraction_-_Charts-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-130">[130]</a></sup> US home mortgage debt relative to GDP increased from an average of 46% during the 1990s to 73% during 2008, reaching $10.5&#160;trillion.<sup id="cite_ref-money.cnn.com_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-money.cnn.com-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>To offset this decline in consumption and lending capacity, the US government and US Federal Reserve have committed $13.9&#160;trillion, of which $6.8&#160;trillion has been invested or spent, as of June 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> In effect, the Fed has gone from being the "<a href="/wiki/Lender_of_last_resort" title="Lender of last resort">lender of last resort</a>" to the "lender of only resort" for a significant portion of the economy. In some cases the Fed can now be considered the "buyer of last resort".</p>
<p>In November 2008, economist <a href="/wiki/Dean_Baker" title="Dean Baker">Dean Baker</a> observed:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>There is a really good reason for tighter credit. Tens of millions of homeowners who had substantial equity in their homes two years ago have little or nothing today. Businesses are facing the worst downturn since the Great Depression. This matters for credit decisions. A homeowner with equity in her home is very unlikely to default on a car loan or credit card debt. They will draw on this equity rather than lose their car and/or have a default placed on their credit record. On the other hand, a homeowner who has no equity is a serious default risk. In the case of businesses, their creditworthiness depends on their future profits. Profit prospects look much worse in November 2008 than they did in November 2007... While many banks are obviously at the brink, consumers and businesses would be facing a much harder time getting credit right now even if the financial system were rock solid. The problem with the economy is the loss of close to $6&#160;trillion in housing wealth and an even larger amount of stock wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At the heart of the portfolios of many of these institutions were investments whose assets had been derived from bundled home mortgages. Exposure to these mortgage-backed securities, or to the <a href="/wiki/Credit_derivatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit derivatives">credit derivatives</a> used to insure them against failure, caused the collapse or takeover of several key firms such as <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_International_Group" title="American International Group">AIG</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merrill_Lynch" title="Merrill Lynch">Merrill Lynch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/HBOS" title="HBOS">HBOS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lehman_Merrill_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lehman_Merrill-238">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_contagion">European contagion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: European contagion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The crisis rapidly developed and spread into a global economic shock, resulting in a number of European <a href="/wiki/Bank_failure" title="Bank failure">bank failures</a>, declines in various stock indexes, and large reductions in the market value of equities<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph-Evans-Pritchard-2007-07-25_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-Evans-Pritchard-2007-07-25-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both MBS and CDO were purchased by corporate and institutional investors globally. Derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a> also increased the linkage between large financial institutions. Moreover, the <a href="/wiki/Leverage_(finance)" title="Leverage (finance)">de-leveraging</a> of financial institutions, as assets were sold to pay back obligations that could not be refinanced in frozen credit markets, further accelerated the solvency crisis and caused a decrease in international trade.</p>
<p>World political leaders, national ministers of finance and central bank directors coordinated their efforts to reduce fears, but the crisis continued.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> At the end of October 2008 a <a href="/wiki/Currency_crisis" title="Currency crisis">currency crisis</a> developed, with investors transferring vast capital resources into stronger currencies such as the yen, the dollar and the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">Swiss franc</a>, leading many emergent economies to seek aid from the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-landler1_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landler1-243">[243]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fackler1_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fackler1-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_the_global_economy_.28as_of_2009.29">Effects on the global economy (as of 2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Effects on the global economy (as of 2009)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_effects">Global effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Global effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
