<p>Several commentators have suggested that if the liquidity crisis continues, an extended <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> or worse could occur.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> The continuing development of the crisis has prompted fears of a global <a href="/wiki/Economic_collapse" title="Economic collapse">economic collapse</a> although there are now many cautiously optimistic forecasters in addition to some prominent sources who remain negative.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> The financial crisis is likely to yield the biggest banking shakeout since the savings-and-loan meltdown.<sup id="cite_ref-bank-failures_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bank-failures-247">[247]</a></sup> Investment bank <a href="/wiki/UBS_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="UBS AG">UBS</a> stated on October 6 that 2008 would see a clear global recession, with recovery unlikely for at least two years.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> Three days later UBS economists announced that the "beginning of the end" of the crisis had begun, with the world starting to make the necessary actions to fix the crisis: <a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">capital</a> injection by governments; injection made systemically; interest rate cuts to help borrowers. The United Kingdom had started systemic injection, and the world's central banks were now cutting interest rates. UBS emphasized the United States needed to implement systemic injection. UBS further emphasized that this fixes only the financial crisis, but that in economic terms "the worst is still to come".<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> UBS quantified their expected recession durations on October 16: the Eurozone's would last two quarters, the United States' would last three quarters, and the United Kingdom's would last four quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932012_Icelandic_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2008–2012 Icelandic financial crisis">economic crisis in Iceland</a> involved all three of the country's major banks. Relative to the size of its economy, Iceland’s banking collapse is the largest suffered by any country in economic history.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the end of October UBS revised its outlook downwards: the forthcoming recession would be the worst since the <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">early 1980s recession</a> with negative 2009 growth for the US, Eurozone, UK; very limited recovery in 2010; but not as bad as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> reported in June 2009 that US consumption accounted for more than a third of the growth in global consumption between 2000 and 2007. "The US economy has been spending too much and borrowing too much for years and the rest of the world depended on the US consumer as a source of global demand." With a recession in the US and the increased savings rate of US consumers, declines in growth elsewhere have been dramatic. For the first quarter of 2009, the annualized rate of decline in GDP was 14.4% in Germany, 15.2% in Japan, 7.4% in the UK, 18% in Latvia,<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> 9.8% in the Euro area and 21.5% for Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> that had seen strong economic growth saw significant slowdowns. For example, growth forecasts in Cambodia show a fall from more than 10% in 2007 to close to zero in 2009, and Kenya may achieve only 3–4% growth in 2009, down from 7% in 2007. According to the research by the <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a>, reductions in growth can be attributed to falls in trade, commodity prices, investment and <a href="/wiki/Remittances" class="mw-redirect" title="Remittances">remittances</a> sent from migrant workers (which reached a record $251&#160;billion in 2007, but have fallen in many countries since).<sup id="cite_ref-odi.org.uk_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odi.org.uk-255">[255]</a></sup> This has stark implications and has led to a dramatic rise in the number of households living below the poverty line, be it 300,000 in Bangladesh or 230,000 in Ghana.<sup id="cite_ref-odi.org.uk_255-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-odi.org.uk-255">[255]</a></sup> Especially states with a fragile political system have to fear that investors from Western states withdraw their money because of the crisis. Bruno Wenn of the German DEG recommends to provide a sound economic policymaking and good governance to attract new investors<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p>The World Bank reported in February 2009 that the Arab World was far less severely affected by the credit crunch. With generally good balance of payments positions coming into the crisis or with alternative sources of financing for their large current account deficits, such as remittances, Foreign Direct Investment (FDI) or foreign aid, Arab countries were able to avoid going to the market in the latter part of 2008. This group is in the best position to absorb the economic shocks. They entered the crisis in exceptionally strong positions. This gives them a significant cushion against the global downturn. The greatest effect of the global economic crisis will come in the form of lower oil prices, which remains the single most important determinant of economic performance. Steadily declining oil prices would force them to draw down reserves and cut down on investments. Significantly lower oil prices could cause a reversal of economic performance as has been the case in past oil shocks. Initial impact will be seen on public finances and employment for foreign workers.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_economic_effects">US economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: US economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Real_gross_domestic_product">Real gross domestic product</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Real gross domestic product">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The output of goods and services produced by labor and property located in the United States—decreased at an annual rate of approximately 6% in the fourth quarter of 2008 and first quarter of 2009, versus activity in the year-ago periods.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> The US unemployment rate increased to 10.1% by October 2009, the highest rate since 1983 and roughly twice the pre-crisis rate. The average hours per work week declined to 33, the lowest level since the government began collecting the data in 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup> With the decline of gross domestic product came the decline in innovation. With fewer resources to risk in creative destruction, the number of patent applications flat-lined. Compared to the previous 5 years of exponential increases in patent application, this stagnation correlates to the similar drop in GDP during the same time period.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_of_wealth_in_the_US">Distribution of wealth in the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Distribution of wealth in the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_Top1percentUSA.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/2008_Top1percentUSA.png/300px-2008_Top1percentUSA.png" width="300" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/2008_Top1percentUSA.png/450px-2008_Top1percentUSA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/2008_Top1percentUSA.png/600px-2008_Top1percentUSA.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2008_Top1percentUSA.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
US inequality from 1913 to 2008.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Typical American families did not fare as well, nor did those "wealthy-but-not wealthiest" families just beneath the pyramid's top. On the other hand, half of the poorest families did not have wealth declines at all during the crisis. The Federal Reserve surveyed 4,000 households between 2007 and 2009, and found that the total wealth of 63 percent of all Americans declined in that period. 77 percent of the richest families had a decrease in total wealth, while only 50 percent of those on the bottom of the pyramid suffered a decrease.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_economic_projections">Official economic projections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Official economic projections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 3, 2008, the European Commission at <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> predicted for 2009 an extremely weak growth of GDP, by 0.1%, for the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> (France, Germany, Italy, Belgium etc.) and even negative number for the UK (−1.0%), Ireland and Spain. On November 6, the IMF at Washington, D.C., launched numbers predicting a worldwide recession by −0.3% for 2009, averaged over the developed economies. On the same day, the Bank of England and the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, respectively, reduced their interest rates from 4.5% down to 3%, and from 3.75% down to 3.25%. As a consequence, starting from November 2008, several countries launched large "help packages" for their economies.</p>
<p>The US Federal Reserve Open Market Committee release in June 2009 stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>...the pace of economic contraction is slowing. Conditions in financial markets have generally improved in recent months. Household spending has shown further signs of stabilizing but remains constrained by ongoing job losses, lower housing wealth, and tight credit. Businesses are cutting back on fixed investment and staffing but appear to be making progress in bringing inventory stocks into better alignment with sales. Although economic activity is likely to remain weak for a time, the Committee continues to anticipate that policy actions to stabilize financial markets and institutions, fiscal and monetary stimulus, and market forces will contribute to a gradual resumption of sustainable economic growth in a context of price stability.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> Economic projections from the Federal Reserve and Reserve Bank Presidents include a return to typical growth levels (GDP) of 2.5–3% in 2010; an unemployment plateau in 2009 and 2010 around 10% with moderation in 2011; and inflation that remains at typical levels around 1–2%.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_responses">Government responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Government responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_and_short-term_responses">Emergency and short-term responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Emergency and short-term responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis#Responses" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">Subprime mortgage crisis §&#160;Responses</a></div>
<p>The US Federal Reserve and central banks around the world took steps to expand money supplies to avoid the risk of a <a href="/wiki/Deflationary_spiral" class="mw-redirect" title="Deflationary spiral">deflationary spiral</a>, in which lower wages and higher unemployment led to a self-reinforcing decline in global consumption. In addition, governments enacted large fiscal stimulus packages, by borrowing and spending to offset the reduction in private sector demand caused by the crisis. The US Federal Reserve's new and expanded liquidity facilities were intended to enable the central bank to fulfill its traditional lender-of-last-resort role during the crisis while mitigating stigma, broadening the set of institutions with access to liquidity, and increasing the flexibility with which institutions could tap such liquidity.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup></p>
<p>This credit freeze brought the <a href="/wiki/Global_financial_system" title="Global financial system">global financial system</a> to the brink of collapse. The response of the Federal Reserve, the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">European Central Bank</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a> and other central banks was immediate and dramatic. During the last quarter of 2008, these central banks purchased US$2.5&#160;trillion of government debt and troubled private assets from banks. This was the largest liquidity injection into the credit market, and the largest monetary policy action, in world history. Following a model initiated by the <a href="/wiki/2008_United_Kingdom_bank_rescue_package" title="2008 United Kingdom bank rescue package">United Kingdom bank rescue package</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Langley2015_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langley2015-268">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup> the governments of European nations and the US guaranteed the debt issued by their banks and raised the capital of their national banking systems, ultimately purchasing $1.5&#160;trillion newly issued <a href="/wiki/Preferred_stock" title="Preferred stock">preferred stock</a> in their major banks.<sup id="cite_ref-foreignaffairs1_227-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreignaffairs1-227">[227]</a></sup> In October 2010, Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a> explained how the US Federal Reserve was implementing another monetary policy —creating currency— as a method to combat the <a href="/wiki/Liquidity_trap" title="Liquidity trap">liquidity trap</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> By creating $600 billion and inserting<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> this directly into banks, the Federal Reserve intended to spur banks to finance more domestic loans and refinance mortgages. However, banks instead were spending the money in more profitable areas by investing internationally in emerging markets. Banks were also investing in foreign currencies, which Stiglitz and others point out may lead to <a href="/wiki/Currency_war" title="Currency war">currency wars</a> while China redirects its currency holdings away from the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup></p>
<p>Governments have also <a href="/wiki/Bailout" title="Bailout">bailed out</a> a variety of firms as discussed above, incurring large financial obligations. To date, various US government agencies have committed or spent trillions of dollars in loans, asset purchases, guarantees, and direct spending.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup> Significant controversy has accompanied the bailout, leading to the development of a variety of "decision making frameworks", to help balance competing policy interests during times of financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US executed two stimulus packages, totaling nearly $1&#160;trillion during 2008 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Other countries also implemented <a href="/wiki/National_fiscal_policy_response_to_the_Great_Recession" title="National fiscal policy response to the Great Recession">fiscal stimulus plans beginning in 2008</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulatory_proposals_and_long-term_responses">Regulatory proposals and long-term responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Regulatory proposals and long-term responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Obama_financial_regulatory_reform_plan_of_2009" class="mw-redirect" title="Obama financial regulatory reform plan of 2009">Obama financial regulatory reform plan of 2009</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_responses_to_the_subprime_crisis" title="Regulatory responses to the subprime crisis">Regulatory responses to the subprime crisis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis_solutions_debate" title="Subprime mortgage crisis solutions debate">Subprime mortgage crisis solutions debate</a></div>
<p>United States President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and key advisers introduced a series of regulatory proposals in June 2009. The proposals address consumer protection, <a href="/wiki/Executive_pay" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive pay">executive pay</a>, bank financial cushions or capital requirements, expanded regulation of the <a href="/wiki/Shadow_banking_system" title="Shadow banking system">shadow banking system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>, and enhanced authority for the Federal Reserve to safely wind-down systemically important institutions, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> In January 2010, Obama proposed additional regulations limiting the ability of banks to engage in <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_trading" title="Proprietary trading">proprietary trading</a>. The proposals were dubbed "The <a href="/wiki/Volcker_Rule" title="Volcker Rule">Volcker Rule</a>", in recognition of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Volcker" title="Paul Volcker">Paul Volcker</a>, who has publicly argued for the proposed changes.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-David_Cho.2C_and_Binyamin_Appelbaum_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_Cho.2C_and_Binyamin_Appelbaum-279">[279]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US Senate passed a reform bill in May 2010, following the House, which passed a bill in December 2009. These bills must now be reconciled. The <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> provided a comparative summary of the features of the two bills, which address to varying extent the principles enumerated by the Obama administration.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> For instance, the Volcker Rule against proprietary trading is not part of the legislation, though in the Senate bill regulators have the discretion but not the obligation to prohibit these trades.</p>
<p>European regulators introduced <a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Basel III</a> regulations for banks.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> It increased capital ratios, limits on leverage, narrow definition of capital (to exclude subordinated debt), limit counter-party risk, and new liquidity requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> Critics argue that Basel III doesn’t address the problem of faulty risk-weightings. Major banks suffered losses from AAA-rated created by <a href="/wiki/Financial_engineering" title="Financial engineering">financial engineering</a> (which creates apparently risk-free assets out of high risk collateral) that required less capital according to Basel II. Lending to AA-rated sovereigns has a risk-weight of zero, thus increasing lending to governments and leading to the next crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johan_Norberg" title="Johan Norberg">Johan Norberg</a> argues that regulations (Basel III among others) have indeed led to excessive lending to risky governments (see <a href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">European sovereign-debt crisis</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/European_Central_Bank" title="European Central Bank">ECB</a> pursues even more lending as the solution.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_Congress_response">United States Congress response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: United States Congress response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At least two major reports were produced by Congress: the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission" title="Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission">Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission</a> report, released January 2011, and a report by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Homeland_Security_Permanent_Subcommittee_on_Investigations" title="United States Senate Homeland Security Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations">United States Senate Homeland Security Permanent Subcommittee on Investigations</a> entitled <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_and_the_Financial_Crisis:_Anatomy_of_a_Financial_Collapse" title="Wall Street and the Financial Crisis: Anatomy of a Financial Collapse">Wall Street and the Financial Crisis: Anatomy of a Financial Collapse</a> (released April 2011).</p>
<ul>
<li>On December 11, 2009 – House cleared bill H.R.4173 – Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup></li>
<li>On April 15, 2010 – Senate introduced bill S.3217 – <a href="/wiki/Restoring_American_Financial_Stability_Act_of_2010" class="mw-redirect" title="Restoring American Financial Stability Act of 2010">Restoring American Financial Stability Act of 2010</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup></li>
<li>On July 21, 2010 – the <a href="/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" title="Dodd–Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd–Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</a> was enacted.<sup id="cite_ref-actions_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-actions-287">[287]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Court_proceedings">Court proceedings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Court proceedings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Iceland in April 2012, the special <a href="/wiki/Landsd%C3%B3mur" class="mw-redirect" title="Landsdómur">Landsdómur</a> court convicted former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Geir_Haarde" title="Geir Haarde">Geir Haarde</a> of mishandling the <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%932012_Icelandic_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2008–2012 Icelandic financial crisis">2008–2012 Icelandic financial crisis</a>.</p>
<p>As of September 2011, no individuals in the UK have been prosecuted for misdeeds during the financial meltdown of 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Continuation_of_the_financial_crisis_in_the_US_housing_market">Continuation of the financial crisis in the US housing market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Continuation of the financial crisis in the US housing market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2012, in the United States, a large volume of troubled mortgages remained in place. It had proved impossible for most homeowners facing foreclosure to refinance or modify their mortgages and foreclosure rates remained high.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT81920_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT81920-290">[290]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stabilization">Stabilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Stabilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The US recession that began in December 2007 ended in June 2009, according to the US <a href="/wiki/National_Bureau_of_Economic_Research" title="National Bureau of Economic Research">National Bureau of Economic Research</a> (NBER)<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup> and the financial crisis appears to have ended about the same time. In April 2009 <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">TIME</a></i> magazine declared "More Quickly Than It Began, The Banking Crisis Is Over."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-292">[292]</a></sup> The United States Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission dates the crisis to 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Crisis_Inquiry_Commission-293">[293]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Financial_Crisis_Was_Avoidable_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Crisis_Was_Avoidable-294">[294]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> declared on January 27, 2010, "the markets are now stabilized, and we've recovered most of the money we spent on the banks."<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> identifies March 2009 as the "nadir of the crisis" and noted in 2011 that "Most stock markets around the world are at least 75 percent higher than they were then. Financial stocks, which led the markets down, have also led them up." Nevertheless, the lack of fundamental changes in banking and financial markets, worries many market participants, including the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Over_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Over-296">[296]</a></sup></p>
<p>The distribution of household incomes in the United States has become more unequal during the post-2008 <a href="/wiki/Economic_recovery" title="Economic recovery">economic recovery</a>, a first for the US but in line with the trend over the last ten economic recoveries since 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Appelbaum_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Appelbaum-298">[298]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Income inequality in the United States">Income inequality in the United States</a> has grown from 2005 to 2012 in more than 2 out of 3 metropolitan areas.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> Median household wealth fell 35% in the US, from $106,591 to $68,839 between 2005 and 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtzleben_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtzleben-300">[300]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_coverage">Media coverage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Media coverage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The financial crises generated many articles and books outside of the scholarly and financial press, including articles and books by author <a href="/wiki/William_Greider" title="William Greider">William Greider</a>, economist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hudson_(economist)" title="Michael Hudson (economist)">Michael Hudson</a>, author and former bond salesman <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis" title="Michael Lewis">Michael Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Phillips_(political_commentator)" title="Kevin Phillips (political commentator)">Kevin Phillips</a>, and investment broker <a href="/wiki/Peter_Schiff" title="Peter Schiff">Peter Schiff</a>.</p>
<p>In May 2010, a documentary, <i>Overdose: A Film about the Next Financial Crisis</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup> premiered about how the financial crisis came about and how the solutions that have been applied by many governments are setting the stage for the next crisis. The film is based on the book <i>Financial Fiasco</i> by Johan Norberg and features <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a>, with funding from the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> think tank <a href="/wiki/The_Cato_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="The Cato Institute">The Cato Institute</a>. Greenspan is responsible for de-regulating the derivatives market while chairman of the Federal Reserve.</p>
<p>In October 2010, a documentary film about the crisis, <i><a href="/wiki/Inside_Job_(2010_film)" title="Inside Job (2010 film)">Inside Job</a></i> directed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Ferguson_(filmmaker)" title="Charles Ferguson (filmmaker)">Charles Ferguson</a>, was released by <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Classics" title="Sony Pictures Classics">Sony Pictures Classics</a>. In 2011, it was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Documentary_Feature" title="Academy Award for Best Documentary Feature">Academy Award for Best Documentary Feature</a> at the <a href="/wiki/83rd_Academy_Awards" title="83rd Academy Awards">83rd Academy Awards</a>.</p>
<p><i>Time</i> magazine named "25 People to Blame for the Financial Crisis".<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Lewis" title="Michael Lewis">Michael Lewis</a> published a best-selling non-fiction book about the crisis, entitled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Big_Short" title="The Big Short">The Big Short</a></i>. In 2015, it was adapted into a <a href="/wiki/The_Big_Short_(film)" title="The Big Short (film)">film of the same name</a>, which won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Adapted_Screenplay" title="Academy Award for Best Adapted Screenplay">Academy Award for Best Adapted Screenplay</a>. One point raised is to what extent those outside of the markets themselves (i.e., not working for a mainstream investment bank) could forecast the events and be generally less myopic. Subsequent to the crisis itself some observers furthermore noted a change in social relations as some group culpability emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emerging_and_developing_economies_drive_global_economic_growth">Emerging and developing economies drive global economic growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Emerging and developing economies drive global economic growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Advanced economies led global economic growth prior to the financial crisis with "emerging" and "developing" economies lagging behind. The crisis overturned this relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> found that "advanced" economies accounted for only 31% of global GDP while emerging and developing economies accounted for 69% of global GDP from 2007 to 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup> In the tables, the names of emergent economies are shown in boldface type, while the names of developed economies are in Roman (regular) type.</p>
